Given: an equation. Strategy: Set $y = 0$ and solve for $x$.

2.15.8 "By how much does $y$ change when $x$ increases by $k$?"

Given: a slope $m$ and a change in $x$ of $k$ units. Strategy: Multiply: $\Delta y = m \cdot k$.

Example: If the slope is $-\frac{3}{2}$, by how much does $y$ change when $x$ increases by 6?

$$\Delta y = -\frac{3}{2} \cdot 6 = -9$$

$y$ decreases by 9.

2.16 Desmos Calculator Strategies for Linear Functions

The built-in Desmos calculator on the Digital SAT is an extraordinarily powerful tool for linear function problems. Here are the key strategies:

2.16.1 Graphing a Line to Find Intercepts

Type the equation (e.g., $y = 3x - 7$) into Desmos. Hover over the $x$-intercept and $y$-intercept to see their coordinates. This is faster and more accurate than solving algebraically for most students.

2.16.2 Finding the Intersection of Two Lines

Type both equations into Desmos. Click on the intersection point to see its coordinates. This solves any system of two linear equations instantly.

2.16.3 Verifying Whether a Point Lies on a Line

Type the equation, then type the point as a coordinate (e.g., $(3, 5)$). If the point appears on the line, it satisfies the equation.

2.16.4 Converting Between Forms

Type the equation in any form. Desmos will graph it regardless. You can also type $y = mx + b$ with sliders for $m$ and $b$ to explore how changes affect the graph.

2.16.5 Finding Slope from Two Points

Type both points. Visually inspect the rise and run. Or, type the slope formula directly: $m = (y_2 - y_1)/(x_2 - x_1)$ with the coordinates substituted.

2.16.6 Table Feature

Desmos allows you to create a table of values for any function. Type the equation, then add a table, and Desmos will populate $y$-values for given $x$-values. This is useful for checking your work or for problems that present partial tables.

2.17 The Intersection of Linear Functions and Geometry

Linear functions do not exist in isolation on the SAT. They frequently intersect with geometry:

Lines on the Coordinate Plane

  • The equation of a line can be determined from two points, which might be vertices of a geometric figure.
  • The slope of a side of a triangle or quadrilateral can be found using the coordinates of its vertices.
  • Perpendicularity of sides (e.g., in a right triangle or rectangle) can be verified by checking that the product of slopes equals $-1$.

Linear Functions Describing Geometric Quantities

  • The perimeter of a shape as a function of one of its dimensions is often linear.
  • The area of a shape as a function of one dimension (with others fixed) may be linear or non-linear depending on the shape.
  • Cost as a function of area (e.g., "carpet costs $3 per square foot") is linear.

Distance and Midpoint

While these are covered more fully in the Coordinate Geometry chapter, note that: - The distance between two points $(x_1, y_1)$ and $(x_2, y_2)$ is found using the Pythagorean theorem: $d = \sqrt{(x_2 - x_1)^2 + (y_2 - y_1)^2}$ - The midpoint of a segment connecting two points is $\left(\frac{x_1 + x_2}{2}, \frac{y_1 + y_2}{2}\right)$ - These formulas are essential for problems involving lines and geometric figures on the coordinate plane.

2.18 Linear Functions: The Bridge to Systems

Linear functions are the building blocks of systems of linear equations, which are covered in the next chapter. A system of two linear equations represents two lines, and solving the system means finding their point of intersection. Every concept in this chapter — slope, intercepts, forms of a line — is essential for mastering systems.

The key connections: - One solution: The lines intersect (different slopes). - No solution: The lines are parallel (same slope, different $y$-intercepts). - Infinitely many solutions: The lines are coincident (same slope, same $y$-intercept).

Understanding these relationships deeply — not just memorizing them — is what separates high scorers from average ones.

2.19 Comprehensive Summary of Linear Function Mastery

2.19.1 What You Must Know Cold

  1. Slope formula: $m = \frac{y_2 - y_1}{x_2 - x_1}$
  2. Slope-intercept form: $y = mx + b$ — $m$ is slope, $b$ is $y$-intercept
  3. Point-slope form: $y - y_1 = m(x - x_1)$
  4. Standard form: $Ax + By = C$ — slope is $-\frac{A}{B}$
  5. Horizontal line: $y = k$, slope = $0$
  6. Vertical line: $x = k$, slope = undefined
  7. Parallel lines: Same slope
  8. Perpendicular lines: Slopes multiply to $-1$ (negative reciprocals)
  9. Slope as rate of change: The dependent variable changes by $m$ units for every 1-unit change in the independent variable
  10. $y$-intercept as starting value: The value of $y$ when $x = 0$

2.19.2 Common Errors to Avoid

  1. Mixing up the order of subtraction in the slope formula. Be consistent: $y_2 - y_1$ over $x_2 - x_1$, or $y_1 - y_2$ over $x_1 - x_2$.
  2. Forgetting the negative sign when finding slope from standard form. The slope is $-\frac{A}{B}$, not $\frac{A}{B}$.
  3. Confusing slope and $y$-intercept in word problems. Rate = slope. Starting value = $y$-intercept.
  4. Sign errors in point-slope form. $y - y_1 = m(x - x_1)$ — if $y_1$ is negative, subtracting a negative becomes addition.
  5. Thinking vertical lines have slope zero. They have undefined slope. Horizontal lines have slope zero.
  6. Forgetting that perpendicular slopes must be negative reciprocals. It's not enough to flip the fraction; you must also change the sign.
  7. Not simplifying slope fractions. $\frac{6}{8}$ should be written as $\frac{3}{4}$.
  8. Assuming all straight relationships are linear functions. Vertical lines are not functions.

2.19.3 The Mental Checklist for Any Linear Function Problem

When you encounter a linear function problem on the SAT, run through this checklist:

  • [ ] What form is the equation in? Do I need to convert it?
  • [ ] What is the slope? What does it mean in context?
  • [ ] What is the $y$-intercept? What does it mean in context?
  • [ ] Is the line horizontal or vertical? (Special cases)
  • [ ] Am I being asked about parallel or perpendicular lines?
  • [ ] Can I use Desmos to verify my answer?
  • [ ] Have I checked the signs carefully?
  • [ ] Have I answered the specific question asked (slope vs. intercept vs. equation vs. interpretation)?

This chapter has provided an exhaustive treatment of linear functions as they appear on the SAT. Every formula, every interpretation, every trap, and every strategy has been explored. The next chapter builds directly on this foundation by examining what happens when two linear functions interact — systems of linear equations.

Chapter 3: Systems of Linear Equations — Solving Methods, Analysis, and Word Problem Applications

3.1 — Introduction and Chapter Overview

A system of linear equations is a collection of two or more linear equations involving the same set of variables. In the context of the SAT, you will almost exclusively encounter systems of two equations with two variables (typically $x$ and $y$). The fundamental question at the heart of every systems problem is this: Is there a pair of values $(x, y)$ that simultaneously satisfies every equation in the system? If such a pair exists, it is called the solution to the system, and graphically, it represents the point(s) where the lines intersect.

This chapter provides an exhaustive, rigorous treatment of every method, nuance, and application of systems of linear equations that you will encounter on the SAT. We will begin by classifying the three possible types of solutions, then move into the three primary algebraic solving methods (Substitution, Elimination, and Graphing), followed by a deep analysis of how to determine the number of solutions without fully solving, and finally, an extensive exploration of word problem modeling and interpretation.

3.2 — The Three Possible Outcomes: A Graphical and Algebraic Taxonomy

Before solving any system, it is critical to understand the three possible scenarios that can occur. Every system of two linear equations in two variables falls into exactly one of three categories.

3.2.1 — One Unique Solution (Consistent and Independent)

Graphical Interpretation: Two distinct lines intersect at exactly one point. The coordinates of that intersection point constitute the solution.

Algebraic Signature: The two equations have different slopes. Because the lines are not parallel, they must cross at precisely one location.

Example: $$y = 2x + 1$$ $$y = -x + 4$$

The first line has slope $2$; the second has slope $-1$. Since $2 \neq -1$, these lines intersect at exactly one point. Solving: $2x + 1 = -x + 4 \Rightarrow 3x = 3 \Rightarrow x = 1$, and substituting back gives $y = 3$. The unique solution is $(1, 3)$.

Key Insight for the SAT: This is the most common outcome on the test. When a system has one solution, you will typically be asked to find it, or you will be asked for the value of some expression involving $x$ and $y$ (such as $x + y$, $xy$, or $2x - y$).

3.2.2 — No Solution (Inconsistent System)

Graphical Interpretation: Two parallel lines that never intersect. There is no point $(x, y)$ that lies on both lines simultaneously.

Algebraic Signature: The two equations have the same slope but different $y$-intercepts. The lines are parallel and distinct.

Example: $$y = 3x + 2$$ $$y = 3x - 5$$

Both lines have slope $m = 3$, but the first has $y$-intercept $2$ while the second has $y$-intercept $-5$. These lines are parallel and will never meet. The system has no solution.

Algebraic Confirmation via Elimination: If you attempt to solve, you might write: $$y = 3x + 2$$ $$y = 3x - 5$$

Subtracting the second equation from the first: $$0 = 7$$

This is a contradiction — a false statement. Whenever you obtain a contradiction (such as $0 = 5$ or $3 = -2$), the system has no solution.

Key Insight for the SAT: The test may present a system in standard form and ask, "For what value of $k$ does the system have no solution?" To answer, you must set the slopes equal and the $y$-intercepts unequal, then solve for the parameter.

3.2.3 — Infinitely Many Solutions (Dependent System)

Graphical Interpretation: The two equations represent the exact same line. Every point on the line is a solution, so there are infinitely many solutions.

Algebraic Signature: The two equations have the same slope AND the same $y$-intercept. The lines are coincident (they lie on top of each other).

Example: $$y = 4x - 7$$ $$8x - 2y = 14$$

At first glance, these look different. But solving the second equation for $y$: $$-2y = -8x + 14$$ $$y = 4x - 7$$

This is identical to the first equation. The two equations describe the same line. The system has infinitely many solutions.

Algebraic Confirmation via Elimination: If you subtract appropriately, you obtain: $$0 = 0$$

This is an identity — a statement that is always true. Whenever you obtain an identity (such as $0 = 0$ or $5 = 5$), the system has infinitely many solutions.

Key Insight for the SAT: Questions about infinitely many solutions often involve a parameter. For example: "What value of $a$ makes the system $2x + 3y = 6$ and $ax + 9y = 18$ dependent?" The answer requires that all coefficients be proportional: $\frac{a}{2} = \frac{9}{3} = \frac{18}{6} = 3$, so $a = 6$.

3.2.4 — Summary Table of the Three Cases

Case Slopes $y$-Intercepts Graph Algebraic Result Number of Solutions
Consistent, Independent Different (irrelevant) Intersecting lines Valid $(x, y)$ pair Exactly 1
Inconsistent Same Different Parallel lines Contradiction ($0 = c$, $c \neq 0$) 0
Dependent Same Same Coincident lines Identity ($0 = 0$) Infinitely many

3.3 — The Analysis Method: Determining the Number of Solutions Without Solving

On the SAT, you are frequently asked not to solve a system but to analyze it — specifically, to determine how many solutions it exists. The fastest and most reliable method is the Analysis Method, which involves converting both equations to slope-intercept form ($y = mx + b$) and comparing the slope ($m$) and $y$-intercept ($b$) of each.

3.3.1 — Step-by-Step Analysis Protocol

Step 1: Convert both equations to $y = mx + b$ form. This may require distributing, combining like terms, and dividing.

Step 2: Identify the slope $m_1$ and $y$-intercept $b_1$ of the first equation, and $m_2$ and $b_2$ of the second.

Step 3: Compare: - If $m_1 \neq m_2$: One solution (the lines intersect). - If $m_1 = m_2$ and $b_1 \neq b_2$: No solution (parallel lines). - If $m_1 = m_2$ and $b_1 = b_2$: Infinitely many solutions (same line).

3.3.2 — Worked Analysis Example

System: $$4x + 2y = 8$$ $$6x + 3y = 15$$

Step 1 — Convert to slope-intercept form:

First equation: $$2y = -4x + 8$$ $$y = -2x + 4$$

Second equation: $$3y = -6x + 15$$ $$y = -2x + 5$$

Step 2 — Identify: - First: $m_1 = -2$, $b_1 = 4$ - Second: $m_2 = -2$, $b_2 = 5$

Step 3 — Compare: - $m_1 = m_2 = -2$ (same slope) - $b_1 = 4 \neq 5 = b_2$ (different $y$-intercepts)

Conclusion: The system has no solution. The lines are parallel.

3.3.3 — The Proportionality Shortcut (Standard Form)

When both equations are in standard form $Ax + By = C$, you can compare ratios without converting:

For the system: $$A_1x + B_1y = C_1$$ $$A_2x + B_2y = C_2$$

  • One solution if $\frac{A_1}{A_2} \neq \frac{B_1}{B_2}$ (slopes differ)
  • No solution if $\frac{A_1}{A_2} = \frac{B_1}{B_2} \neq \frac{C_1}{C_2}$ (same slope, different intercepts)
  • Infinitely many solutions if $\frac{A_1}{A_2} = \frac{B_1}{B_2} = \frac{C_1}{C_2}$ (all proportional)

Applied to the previous example: $$\frac{4}{6} = \frac{2}{3}, \quad \frac{2}{3} = \frac{2}{3}, \quad \frac{8}{15} \neq \frac{2}{3}$$

Since $\frac{4}{6} = \frac{2}{3} = \frac{2}{3} \neq \frac{8}{15}$, we confirm: no solution.

3.4 — Solving Method 1: Substitution

The substitution method is most effective when at least one of the equations has a variable with a coefficient of $\pm1$. This makes it easy to isolate that variable and substitute its expression into the other equation.

3.4.1 — The Substitution Algorithm

Step 1: Choose the equation and variable that is easiest to isolate (ideally, a variable with coefficient $\pm1$).

Step 2: Solve that equation for the chosen variable, obtaining an expression in terms of the other variable.

Step 3: Substitute this expression into the other equation. This produces a single equation in one variable.

Step 4: Solve the resulting one-variable equation.

Step 5: Substitute the found value back into the expression from Step 2 to find the other variable.

Step 6: State the solution as an ordered pair $(x, y)$.

3.4.2 — Worked Example: Clean Substitution

System: $$y = 2x + 1$$ $$3x + 2y = 16$$

Step 1: The first equation already has $y$ isolated.

Step 2: $y = 2x + 1$ (expression ready).

Step 3: Substitute into the second equation: $$3x + 2(2x + 1) = 16$$

Step 4: Solve: $$3x + 4x + 2 = 16$$ $$7x + 2 = 16$$ $$7x = 14$$ $$x = 2$$

Step 5: Substitute $x = 2$ into $y = 2x + 1$: $$y = 2(2) + 1 = 5$$

Step 6: The solution is $(2, 5)$.

Verification: Check in the second equation: $3(2) + 2(5) = 6 + 10 = 16$ ✓

3.4.3 — Worked Example: Rearrangement Required

System: $$4x - y = 7$$ $$x + 3y = 11$$

Step 1: The first equation has $y$ with coefficient $-1$, making it easy to isolate $y$.

Step 2: From $4x - y = 7$: $$-y = -4x + 7$$ $$y = 4x - 7$$

Step 3: Substitute into the second equation: $$x + 3(4x - 7) = 11$$

Step 4: Solve: $$x + 12x - 21 = 11$$ $$13x - 21 = 11$$ $$13x = 32$$ $$x = \frac{32}{13}$$

Step 5: Substitute back: $$y = 4\left(\frac{32}{13}\right) - 7 = \frac{128}{13} - \frac{91}{13} = \frac{37}{13}$$

Step 6: The solution is $\left(\frac{32}{13}, \frac{37}{13}\right)$.

Key Insight: The SAT often designs problems so that the solution is a clean integer or simple fraction. If you find yourself with messy fractions, double-check your arithmetic before proceeding.

3.4.4 — When Substitution Is Inefficient

If neither equation has a variable with coefficient $\pm1$, substitution still works but involves more fraction manipulation. For example:

$$3x + 4y = 10$$ $$5x + 2y = 8$$

Here, isolating any variable introduces fractions. While substitution is still valid, the elimination method (covered next) is generally faster and less error-prone for such systems.

3.5 — Solving Method 2: Elimination (Linear Combination)

The elimination method is the most versatile and widely applicable technique. It is especially powerful when no variable has coefficient $\pm1$ or when both equations are in standard form.

3.5.1 — The Elimination Algorithm

Step 1: Align both equations in the same form (preferably standard form $Ax + By = C$).

Step 2: Choose a variable to eliminate. Look for the variable whose coefficients are easiest to match (ideally, the least common multiple of the coefficients is small).

Step 3: Multiply one or both equations by constants so that the coefficients of the chosen variable are opposites (e.g., $5$ and $-5$).

Step 4: Add the two equations vertically. The chosen variable should cancel out, leaving a single equation in one variable.

Step 5: Solve the resulting one-variable equation.

Step 6: Substitute the found value back into either original equation to find the other variable.

Step 7: State the solution as an ordered pair $(x, y)$.

3.5.2 — Worked Example: Simple Elimination

System: $$2x + 3y = 12$$ $$4x - 3y = 6$$

Step 1: Both equations are already in standard form.

Step 2: The $y$-coefficients are $3$ and $-3$ — they are already opposites! No multiplication needed.

Step 3: (Skip — coefficients are already opposites.)

Step 4: Add the equations: $$\begin{array}{r} 2x + 3y = 12 \ +\quad 4x - 3y = 6 \ \hline 6x = 18 \end{array}$$

Step 5: Solve: $$x = 3$$

Step 6: Substitute $x = 3$ into the first equation: $$2(3) + 3y = 12$$ $$6 + 3y = 12$$ $$3y = 6$$ $$y = 2$$

Step 7: The solution is $(3, 2)$.

Verification: Check in the second equation: $4(3) - 3(2) = 12 - 6 = 6$ ✓

3.5.3 — Worked Example: Multiplication Required

System: $$3x + 2y = 14$$ $$5x + 3y = 23$$

Step 1: Both in standard form.

Step 2: Let's eliminate $y$. The coefficients are $2$ and $3$. The LCM of $2$ and $3$ is $6$.

Step 3: Multiply the first equation by $3$ and the second by $-2$: $$9x + 6y = 42$$ $$-10x - 6y = -46$$

Step 4: Add: $$\begin{array}{r} 9x + 6y = 42 \ +\quad -10x - 6y = -46 \ \hline -x = -4 \end{array}$$

Step 5: Solve: $$x = 4$$

Step 6: Substitute $x = 4$ into the first original equation: $$3(4) + 2y = 14$$ $$12 + 2y = 14$$ $$2y = 2$$ $$y = 1$$

Step 7: The solution is $(4, 1)$.

Verification: Check in the second equation: $5(4) + 3(1) = 20 + 3 = 23$ ✓

3.5.4 — Strategic Choice of Variable to Eliminate

A key strategic decision in elimination is which variable to eliminate. The general principle is: choose the variable that requires the least amount of multiplication. Look for:

  1. Already opposite coefficients (e.g., $4$ and $-4$): Add immediately. No multiplication needed.
  2. Same coefficients (e.g., $3$ and $3$): Multiply one equation by $-1$, then add.
  3. One coefficient is a multiple of the other (e.g., $2$ and $6$): Multiply the equation with the smaller coefficient by the appropriate factor.
  4. Neither is a multiple (e.g., $3$ and $5$): Use the LCM approach, but consider whether eliminating the other variable would be simpler.

3.5.5 — Elimination Revealing Special Cases

Elimination is also the method that most clearly reveals whether a system has no solution or infinitely many solutions.

No Solution Example: $$2x + 3y = 7$$ $$4x + 6y = 13$$

Multiply the first equation by $-2$: $$-4x - 6y = -14$$ $$4x + 6y = 13$$

Add: $$0 = -1$$

Contradiction. No solution.

Infinitely Many Solutions Example: $$3x - 2y = 8$$ $$6x - 4y = 16$$

Multiply the first equation by $-2$: $$-6x + 4y = -16$$ $$6x - 4y = 16$$

Add: $$0 = 0$$

Identity. Infinitely many solutions.

3.6 — Solving Method 3: Graphing

The graphical method involves plotting both lines and identifying their intersection point. While this is the least precise method for hand calculation, it is conceptually vital and is directly supported by the Desmos graphing calculator available on the Digital SAT.

3.6.1 — The Graphing Algorithm

Step 1: Convert both equations to slope-intercept form ($y = mx + b$).

Step 2: Identify the slope and $y$-intercept of each line.

Step 3: Plot the $y$-intercept of the first line, then use the slope to find a second point. Draw the line.

Step 4: Repeat for the second line.

Step 5: Identify the intersection point. Its coordinates are the solution.

3.6.2 — Using Desmos for Graphing

On the Digital SAT, you can use the built-in Desmos calculator to graph both equations and find their intersection. This is often the fastest method for multiple-choice questions.

Procedure in Desmos: 1. Type both equations into the expression lines. 2. Desmos will graph both lines. 3. Click on the intersection point. Desmos will display the coordinates. 4. Select the answer choice that matches.

Critical Note: Desmos shows coordinates as decimals. If the answer choices are fractions, you may need to convert. For example, Desmos might show $(0.667, 1.333)$, which corresponds to $\left(\frac{2}{3}, \frac{4}{3}\right)$.

3.6.3 — Graphical Interpretation of Special Cases

  • One solution: The two lines cross at one point.
  • No solution: The two lines are parallel (same slope, different intercepts) and never touch.
  • Infinitely many solutions: The two lines are the same line (coincident).

3.7 — Comparative Analysis: Choosing the Best Method

Not all systems are created equal. The optimal method depends on the structure of the equations. Here is a comprehensive decision framework:

System Structure Best Method Why
One equation has $y$ (or $x$) isolated or with coefficient $\pm1$ Substitution Minimal rearrangement needed
Both equations in standard form; no variable has coefficient $\pm1$ Elimination Avoids fractions
Coefficients of one variable are opposites Elimination (add immediately) Fastest possible elimination
Coefficients of one variable are equal Elimination (multiply one by $-1$) Very fast elimination
System involves parameters (e.g., "find $k$ for no solution") Analysis Method Compare slopes/intercepts directly
Multiple-choice with messy numbers Desmos/Graphing Visual and fast
Asked for $x + y$ or $xy$ rather than individual values Strategic Elimination May find the combination directly

3.7.1 — The "Find the Combination" Shortcut

Sometimes the SAT asks not for $x$ or $y$ individually, but for a combination like $x + y$, $x - y$, or $3x + 2y$. In such cases, you may be able to find the answer without solving for $x$ and $y$ separately.

Example: $$2x + 3y = 12$$ $$3x + 2y = 13$$

Question: What is the value of $x + y$?

Solution: Add the two equations: $$5x + 5y = 25$$ $$5(x + y) = 25$$ $$x + y = 5$$

The answer is $5$, found without ever determining $x$ or $y$ individually.

Another Example: $$4x - y = 10$$ $$2x + y = 8$$

Question: What is the value of $x$?

Solution: Add the equations: $$6x = 18$$ $$x = 3$$

This is faster than solving for both variables.

3.8 — Systems Involving Parameters

A sophisticated SAT question type involves a system with an unknown constant (parameter) and asks you to determine the value of that parameter based on the number of solutions.

3.8.1 — Finding the Parameter for No Solution

Principle: For no solution, the lines must be parallel (same slope) but not coincident (different $y$-intercepts).

Example: $$2x + ky = 8$$ $$4x + 6y = 12$$

Question: What value of $k$ makes the system have no solution?

Solution:

Convert both to slope-intercept form:

First equation: $$ky = -2x + 8$$ $$y = -\frac{2}{k}x + \frac{8}{k}$$

Second equation: $$6y = -4x + 12$$ $$y = -\frac{4}{6}x + 2$$ $$y = -\frac{2}{3}x + 2$$

For no solution, the slopes must be equal: $$-\frac{2}{k} = -\frac{2}{3}$$ $$\frac{2}{k} = \frac{2}{3}$$ $$k = 3$$

Now check the $y$-intercepts: First equation has $y$-intercept $\frac{8}{3}$; second has $2 = \frac{6}{3}$. Since $\frac{8}{3} \neq \frac{6}{3}$, the lines are parallel and distinct. $k = 3$ is the answer.

3.8.2 — Finding the Parameter for Infinitely Many Solutions

Principle: For infinitely many solutions, the two equations must represent the same line. All coefficients must be proportional.

Example: $$ax + 3y = 9$$ $$4x + 6y = 18$$

Question: What value of $a$ makes the system have infinitely many solutions?

Solution:

For the equations to be identical, the ratios of corresponding coefficients must be equal: $$\frac{a}{4} = \frac{3}{6} = \frac{9}{18}$$

Since $\frac{3}{6} = \frac{1}{2}$ and $\frac{9}{18} = \frac{1}{2}$, we need: $$\frac{a}{4} = \frac{1}{2}$$ $$a = 2$$

Answer: $a = 2$.

3.8.3 — The Proportionality Method for Standard Form

For a system in standard form: $$A_1x + B_1y = C_1$$ $$A_2x + B_2y = C_2$$

  • No solution: $\frac{A_1}{A_2} = \frac{B_1}{B_2} \neq \frac{C_1}{C_2}$
  • Infinitely many solutions: $\frac{A_1}{A_2} = \frac{B_1}{B_2} = \frac{C_1}{C_2}$

This method avoids converting to slope-intercept form entirely.

3.9 — Word Problem Applications: Modeling Real-World Scenarios

The SAT frequently presents systems of equations in the context of word problems. The challenge is twofold: (1) translating the verbal description into mathematical equations, and (2) solving the resulting system.

3.9.1 — The Translation Framework

Step 1: Define your variables. Write a clear statement like "Let $x$ = the number of adult tickets and $y$ = the number of child tickets."

Step 2: Identify the two independent pieces of information that will become your two equations. Common patterns include: - A total count equation (e.g., total number of items) - A total value equation (e.g., total cost, total revenue) - A relationship equation (e.g., one quantity is twice another)

Step 3: Write both equations.

Step 4: Solve using the most appropriate method.

Step 5: Answer the specific question asked (which may not simply be "find $x$ and $y$").

3.9.2 — Ticket and Pricing Problems

Example Scenario: A movie theater sells adult tickets for $\$12$ and child tickets for $\$8$. On a particular day, 250 tickets were sold, generating $\$2,600$ in revenue. How many adult tickets were sold?

Solution:

Step 1: Let $a$ = number of adult tickets, $c$ = number of child tickets.

Step 2-3: Two equations: $$a + c = 250 \quad \text{(total tickets)}$$ $$12a + 8c = 2600 \quad \text{(total revenue)}$$

Step 4: Solve by substitution (since $a$ has coefficient $1$ in the first equation): $$c = 250 - a$$

Substitute into the second equation: $$12a + 8(250 - a) = 2600$$ $$12a + 2000 - 8a = 2600$$ $$4a + 2000 = 2600$$ $$4a = 600$$ $$a = 150$$

Step 5: The number of adult tickets sold is 150.

Verification: If $a = 150$, then $c = 100$. Revenue: $12(150) + 8(100) = 1800 + 800 = 2600$ ✓

3.9.3 — Mixture and Blend Problems

Example Scenario: A coffee shop blends two types of coffee: Type A costs $\$6$ per pound, and Type B costs $\$9$ per pound. How many pounds of each type should be mixed to create 50 pounds of a blend worth $\$7.80$ per pound?

Solution:

Step 1: Let $x$ = pounds of Type A, $y$ = pounds of Type B.

Step 2-3: Two equations: $$x + y = 50 \quad \text{(total weight)}$$ $$6x + 9y = 7.80(50) = 390 \quad \text{(total cost)}$$

Step 4: Solve by substitution: $$y = 50 - x$$ $$6x + 9(50 - x) = 390$$ $$6x + 450 - 9x = 390$$ $$-3x + 450 = 390$$ $$-3x = -60$$ $$x = 20$$

Then $y = 50 - 20 = 30$.

Step 5: The blend should contain 20 pounds of Type A and 30 pounds of Type B.

Verification: Cost: $6(20) + 9(30) = 120 + 270 = 390$. Total weight: $20 + 30 = 50$. Price per pound: $390/50 = 7.80$ ✓

3.9.4 — Rate and Distance Problems

Example Scenario: Two cyclists start from the same point. Cyclist A rides east at 15 mph, and Cyclist B rides west at 12 mph. After how many hours will they be 81 miles apart?

Solution:

Step 1: Let $t$ = time in hours.

Step 2-3: Distance = Rate × Time. Since they ride in opposite directions, the total distance between them is the sum of their individual distances: $$d_A = 15t, \quad d_B = 12t$$ $$d_A + d_B = 81$$ $$15t + 12t = 81$$

Step 4: Solve: $$27t = 81$$ $$t = 3$$

Step 5: They will be 81 miles apart after 3 hours.

3.9.5 — Break-Even Analysis

Example Scenario: A company produces widgets. The fixed cost is $\$500$, and the variable cost per widget is $\$3$. Each widget sells for $\$8$. How many widgets must be produced and sold to break even?

Solution:

Step 1: Let $x$ = number of widgets.

Step 2-3: At break-even, Total Cost = Total Revenue. $$\text{Total Cost} = 500 + 3x$$ $$\text{Total Revenue} = 8x$$ $$500 + 3x = 8x$$

Step 4: Solve: $$500 = 5x$$ $$x = 100$$

Step 5: The company must produce and sell 100 widgets to break even.

3.9.6 — Age Problems

Example Scenario: A father is currently 3 times as old as his son. In 12 years, the father will be twice as old as his son. How old is the son now?

Solution:

Step 1: Let $f$ = father's current age, $s$ = son's current age.

Step 2-3: Two equations: $$f = 3s \quad \text{(current relationship)}$$ $$f + 12 = 2(s + 12) \quad \text{(future relationship)}$$

Step 4: Substitute $f = 3s$ into the second equation: $$3s + 12 = 2(s + 12)$$ $$3s + 12 = 2s + 24$$ $$s = 12$$

Step 5: The son is currently 12 years old.

Verification: Father is $3(12) = 36$. In 12 years: father will be 48, son will be 24. And $48 = 2(24)$ ✓

3.10 — Systems in Context: Interpreting the Solution

On the SAT, you may be given a system already set up in a real-world context and asked to interpret what the solution (or the slope, or the $y$-intercept) means.

3.10.1 — Interpreting the Intersection Point

When two linear models intersect, the $x$-coordinate of the intersection represents the input value at which both models produce the same output value. The $y$-coordinate is that common output.

Example: If $C_1(x) = 50 + 10x$ models the cost of Gym A (where $x$ is months) and $C_2(x) = 20 + 15x$ models the cost of Gym B, then solving $C_1(x) = C_2(x)$ gives $x = 6$, meaning that after 6 months, both gyms cost the same amount ($\$110$).

3.10.2 — Interpreting the Slopes in Context

In a word problem, the slope of each equation represents the rate of change of the dependent variable with respect to the independent variable. Comparing slopes tells you which quantity is growing faster.

3.10.3 — Interpreting the $y$-Intercepts in Context

The $y$-intercept represents the starting value — the value of the dependent variable when the independent variable is zero. In cost problems, this is often a fixed or startup cost. In distance problems, it might represent an initial head start.

3.11 — Advanced System Configurations

3.11.1 — Systems with Three Variables

While rare on the SAT, you may occasionally encounter a system with three equations and three variables. The same principles apply: use elimination to reduce the system to two equations in two variables, then solve.

Example: $$x + y + z = 6$$ $$2x - y + z = 3$$ $$x + 2y - z = 2$$

Strategy: Add the first and third equations to eliminate $z$: $$2x + 3y = 8$$

Add the second and third equations (after multiplying the third by appropriate factor) to eliminate $z$ again, then solve the resulting $2 \times 2$ system.

Note: The SAT almost never requires full $3 \times 3$ solutions. If a three-variable system appears, it is usually designed so that a shortcut (such as adding all three equations) yields the answer directly.

3.11.2 — Nonlinear Systems

Some SAT problems pair a linear equation with a quadratic equation. The solution method is typically substitution.

Example: $$y = x^2 - 4x + 3$$ $$y = x - 1$$

Solution: Set the expressions for $y$ equal: $$x^2 - 4x + 3 = x - 1$$ $$x^2 - 5x + 4 = 0$$ $$(x - 1)(x - 4) = 0$$ $$x = 1 \quad \text{or} \quad x = 4$$

Find corresponding $y$-values: - When $x = 1$: $y = 1 - 1 = 0$. Point: $(1, 0)$. - When $x = 4$: $y = 4 - 1 = 3$. Point: $(4, 3)$.

The system has two solutions: $(1, 0)$ and $(4, 3)$.

Graphical Interpretation: The line intersects the parabola at two points.

3.12 — Common SAT Traps and Misconceptions

3.12.1 — The "No Solution" vs. "Infinitely Many Solutions" Confusion

Students frequently confuse these two cases. Remember: - No solution = parallel lines = contradiction ($0 = 5$) - Infinitely many solutions = same line = identity ($0 = 0$)

The key differentiator is the $y$-intercept (or the constant ratio in standard form). Same slope alone is not enough — you must also check the intercept.

3.12.2 — Solving for the Wrong Variable

After finding $x$, students sometimes forget to find $y$ and instead select the $x$-value as the final answer. Always re-read the question to confirm what is being asked.

3.12.3 — Arithmetic Errors with Negative Signs

Elimination frequently involves subtracting equations or multiplying by negative numbers. A single sign error can derail the entire solution. Best practice: write out every step rather than doing mental math.

3.12.4 — Assuming a System Has a Solution

Some SAT questions ask, "Which of the following is true about the system?" If you blindly solve without first checking whether the system is consistent, you might waste time or reach an incorrect conclusion. Always analyze first.

3.12.5 — Forgetting to Check the Answer

Once you have a solution, substitute both values back into both original equations. This takes 10 seconds and catches most errors.

3.13 — Special SAT Question Types Involving Systems

3.13.1 — "Which System Models This Situation?"

You may be given a word problem and asked to select the correct system of equations from four options. Strategy: Write your own system first, then match it to the answer choices. Alternatively, test each answer choice against the given information.

3.13.2 — "What Is the Value of $x + y$?"

As discussed in Section 3.7.1, adding or subtracting the equations directly may yield the desired combination without solving for individual variables. Always look for this shortcut before resorting to full substitution or elimination.

3.13.3 — "For What Value of $k$ Does the System Have No Solution?"

This is a parameter question. Set the slopes equal and the $y$-intercepts unequal, then solve for $k$. See Section 3.8 for the full methodology.

3.13.4 — "The System Has Infinitely Many Solutions. What Is $a$?"

Set all coefficients proportional and solve for the unknown parameter. See Section 3.8.2.

3.13.5 — Graphical Questions with Desmos

When a question shows a graph of two lines and asks for the solution, you can either read the intersection point from the graph or, if the equations are given, type them into Desmos for a precise answer.

3.14 — Systems and the Digital SAT: Calculator Strategy

The Digital SAT's built-in Desmos calculator is a powerful tool for systems of equations. Here is how to leverage it:

3.14.1 — Finding Intersections with Desmos

  1. Enter both equations as $y = \text{[expression]}$.
  2. Desmos graphs both lines.
  3. Click the intersection point. The coordinates appear.
  4. If the answer is a fraction, Desmos may show a decimal. Use the "fraction" button or convert manually.

3.14.2 — Solving Systems with Sliders

For parameter questions (e.g., "What value of $a$ makes the system have no solution?"), you can create a slider for $a$ in Desmos and adjust it until the lines become parallel. This visual approach can confirm your algebraic answer.

3.14.3 — When NOT to Use Desmos

  • If the system is simple (e.g., $y = 2x + 1$ and $y = -x + 4$), solving by hand is faster than typing into Desmos.
  • If the answer choices are in fraction form and the intersection has a non-terminating decimal, you may need to solve algebraically to get the exact fraction.

3.15 — Comprehensive Method Summary

Substitution Method

  • Best when: A variable has coefficient $\pm1$.
  • Process: Isolate → Substitute → Solve → Back-substitute.
  • Advantage: Direct, intuitive.
  • Disadvantage: Can produce fractions if no variable has coefficient $\pm1$.

Elimination Method

  • Best when: Both equations are in standard form; no variable has coefficient $\pm1$.
  • Process: Match coefficients → Add/subtract → Solve → Back-substitute.
  • Advantage: Avoids fractions in many cases; clearly reveals special cases.
  • Disadvantage: Requires careful multiplication and sign management.

Graphing Method

  • Best when: Using Desmos on the Digital SAT; visual confirmation needed.
  • Process: Graph both lines → Identify intersection.
  • Advantage: Visual; supported by technology.
  • Disadvantage: Less precise for hand-drawn graphs; decimal approximations.

Analysis Method

  • Best when: Determining the number of solutions without solving.
  • Process: Convert to $y = mx + b$ → Compare $m$ and $b$.
  • Advantage: Fastest way to classify a system.
  • Disadvantage: Does not produce the actual solution.

3.16 — Connections to Other Topics

Systems of linear equations do not exist in isolation. They connect to several other areas tested on the SAT:

3.16.1 — Linear Inequalities

A system of linear inequalities defines a feasible region (a shaded area on the graph). The boundary lines are found by solving the corresponding system of equations. The SAT may ask which point lies in the solution region.

3.16.2 — Quadratic-Linear Systems

As discussed in Section 3.11.2, pairing a linear equation with a quadratic yields a system solvable by substitution. The number of solutions (0, 1, or 2) corresponds to the number of intersection points between the line and the parabola.

3.16.3 — Interpreting Graphs

Given a graph of two lines, you can read the solution directly. Given a table of values for two linear functions, you can find where their outputs are equal.

3.16.4 — Slope and Parallel/Perpendicular Analysis

The condition for no solution (same slope) is the same as the condition for parallel lines. Understanding this connection reinforces both topics.

3.17 — Final Notes on Mastery

Mastery of systems of linear equations requires:

  1. Fluency in all three solving methods — You should be able to solve any system by substitution, elimination, or graphing, and you should know which method is fastest for a given system.

  2. Instant recognition of the three cases — Given any system, you should be able to determine (within seconds) whether it has 0, 1, or infinitely many solutions by comparing slopes and intercepts.

  3. Precision with parameters — You should be able to find the value of an unknown constant that produces a specific number of solutions.

  4. Confidence with word problems — You should be able to translate a verbal description into a system, solve it, and interpret the solution in context.

  5. Strategic use of Desmos — On the Digital SAT, you should know when to use the graphing calculator and when to solve by hand.

Systems of linear equations are one of the most heavily tested topics on the SAT Math section, falling under both the "Algebra" and "Advanced Math" content domains. A deep, flexible understanding of this topic will pay dividends across dozens of potential test questions.

End of Chapter 3

Chapter 4: Quadratics Deep Dive — Forms, Transformations, Roots, and the Discriminant

4.1 — The Nature of Quadratic Functions

A quadratic function is a polynomial function of degree 2, meaning the highest exponent on the independent variable is 2. The general shape of a quadratic function's graph is called a parabola — a symmetric, U-shaped (or inverted U-shaped) curve that opens either upward or downward.

Quadratics are among the most heavily tested topics on the SAT Math section, appearing across both the Algebra and Advanced Math domains. They serve as a bridge between linear functions and more complex polynomial behavior, introducing the critical concept of non-constant rate of change.

The Parent Function

The simplest quadratic function is the parent function:

$$f(x) = x^2$$

This parabola has its vertex at the origin $(0, 0)$, opens upward, and is symmetric about the y-axis. Every other quadratic function can be understood as a transformation of this parent function — shifted, stretched, compressed, or reflected.

Key Characteristics of Every Parabola

Before diving into specific forms, it is essential to understand the universal features that every parabola possesses:

  1. Vertex: The single turning point of the parabola — the highest or lowest point on the graph.
  2. Axis of Symmetry: A vertical line passing through the vertex that divides the parabola into two mirror-image halves.
  3. Direction of Opening: Either upward (when the leading coefficient is positive) or downward (when the leading coefficient is negative).
  4. y-intercept: The point where the graph crosses the y-axis (found by evaluating $f(0)$).
  5. x-intercepts (Roots/Zeros): The point(s) where the graph crosses the x-axis (found by solving $f(x) = 0$). A parabola may have 0, 1, or 2 x-intercepts.
  6. Domain: All real numbers, written as $(-\infty, \infty)$ or $\mathbb{R}$.
  7. Range: Restricted — either $[k, \infty)$ if the parabola opens upward, or $(-\infty, k]$ if it opens downward, where $k$ is the y-coordinate of the vertex.

4.2 — The Three Forms of a Quadratic Function

The SAT requires fluency in three distinct but equivalent forms of a quadratic function. Each form reveals different information about the parabola and is optimal for different tasks.

4.2.1 — Standard Form: $f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c$

What it reveals: - The y-intercept is immediately visible as the constant term $c$. The y-intercept occurs at the point $(0, c)$. - The direction of opening is determined by the sign of $a$. - The coefficients $a$, $b$, and $c$ can be used to find the vertex and discriminant.

When to use it: - When the problem gives you three points and asks you to find the equation. - When you need to quickly identify the y-intercept. - When applying the quadratic formula. - When computing the discriminant.

Finding the vertex from Standard Form: The x-coordinate of the vertex is given by:

$$x = -\frac{b}{2a}$$

This formula is derived from completing the square or from the fact that the vertex lies on the axis of symmetry, which is exactly halfway between the two roots (when they exist). Once you have the x-coordinate, substitute it back into the function to find the y-coordinate:

$$f!\left(-\frac{b}{2a}\right) = a!\left(-\frac{b}{2a}\right)^2 + b!\left(-\frac{b}{2a}\right) + c$$

Critical nuance: The formula $x = -\frac{b}{2a}$ includes the negative sign in the numerator. A very common SAT trap is to forget this negative sign. The x-coordinate of the vertex is the opposite of $b$ divided by $2a$.

Example of the trap: For $f(x) = 2x^2 - 8x + 5$, the x-coordinate of the vertex is $x = -(-8)/(2 \cdot 2) = 8/4 = 2$, NOT $-8/4 = -2$.

4.2.2 — Vertex Form: $f(x) = a(x - h)^2 + k$

What it reveals: - The vertex is immediately visible as the point $(h, k)$. - The axis of symmetry is $x = h$. - The direction of opening is determined by the sign of $a$. - The vertical stretch/compression factor is $|a|$.

When to use it: - When the problem explicitly gives you the vertex. - When the problem describes transformations of the parent function. - When you need to quickly identify the maximum or minimum value of the function. - When modeling projectile motion or optimization problems.

The sign trap with h: This is arguably the most common error students make with quadratics. In the expression $(x - h)^2$, the vertex has x-coordinate $h$. But in the expression $(x + 3)^2$, this is actually $(x - (-3))^2$, so $h = -3$, NOT $h = +3$.

Written Form Equivalent Form Value of $h$ Vertex x-coordinate
$(x - 5)^2$ $(x - 5)^2$ $h = 5$ $x = 5$
$(x + 3)^2$ $(x - (-3))^2$ $h = -3$ $x = -3$
$(x - 0)^2$ $x^2$ $h = 0$ $x = 0$
$(x + 7)^2$ $(x - (-7))^2$ $h = -7$ $x = -7$

Why this happens: The vertex form is derived from shifting the parent function $y = x^2$ horizontally by $h$ units. The shift $(x - h)$ moves the graph right by $h$ units when $h > 0$. The shift $(x + 3) = (x - (-3))$ moves the graph left by 3 units.

4.2.3 — Intercept (Factored) Form: $f(x) = a(x - p)(x - q)$

What it reveals: - The x-intercepts (roots) are immediately visible as $x = p$ and $x = q$. - The x-intercepts occur at the points $(p, 0)$ and $(q, 0)$. - The direction of opening is determined by the sign of $a$.

When to use it: - When the problem explicitly gives you the x-intercepts (roots). - When the quadratic is easily factorable and you need to find the roots quickly. - When you need to find the vertex (the x-coordinate is the midpoint of $p$ and $q$).

Finding the vertex from Intercept Form: Since the parabola is symmetric, the axis of symmetry lies exactly halfway between the two x-intercepts. Therefore:

$$x_{\text{vertex}} = \frac{p + q}{2}$$

Substitute this x-value back into the function to find the y-coordinate of the vertex.

The sign trap with p and q: Just as with vertex form, the signs are reversed. In $f(x) = (x - 3)(x + 5)$, the x-intercepts are $x = 3$ and $x = -5$ (NOT $x = -3$ and $x = +5$). This is because setting each factor equal to zero gives $x - 3 = 0 \Rightarrow x = 3$ and $x + 5 = 0 \Rightarrow x = -5$.

4.2.4 — Summary Table: What Each Form Reveals

Form Equation Immediately Reveals Requires Work to Find
Standard $ax^2 + bx + c$ y-intercept $(0, c)$, direction of opening Vertex, roots
Vertex $a(x - h)^2 + k$ Vertex $(h, k)$, axis of symmetry, direction of opening y-intercept, x-intercepts
Intercept $a(x - p)(x - q)$ x-intercepts $(p, 0)$ and $(q, 0)$, direction of opening y-intercept, vertex

4.3 — The Role of the Leading Coefficient $a$

The parameter $a$ appears in all three forms of a quadratic and controls two critical features of the parabola:

4.3.1 — Direction of Opening

  • If $a > 0$: The parabola opens upward. The vertex is the minimum point of the function. The function decreases to the left of the vertex and increases to the right.
  • If $a < 0$: The parabola opens downward. The vertex is the maximum point of the function. The function increases to the left of the vertex and decreases to the right.

4.3.2 — Vertical Stretch and Compression

The absolute value $|a|$ determines how "steep" or "flat" the parabola appears:

  • If $|a| > 1$: The parabola is vertically stretched (narrower than the parent function $y = x^2$). The graph rises more quickly.
  • If $0 < |a| < 1$: The parabola is vertically compressed (wider than the parent function). The graph rises more slowly.
  • If $|a| = 1$: The parabola has the same "width" as the parent function.

4.3.3 — Reflection

  • If $a < 0$: The parabola is reflected across the x-axis compared to the parent function. This is why it opens downward.

4.3.4 — The Invariance of $a$ Across Forms

A crucial insight for the SAT: the value of $a$ is the same in all three forms of the same quadratic function. This means:

  • If $f(x) = 3x^2 - 12x + 11$ (Standard Form), then $a = 3$.
  • If we rewrite it as $f(x) = 3(x - 2)^2 - 1$ (Vertex Form), then $a = 3$.
  • If we rewrite it as $f(x) = 3(x - 3)(x - 1)$ (Intercept Form — note: this is a different function, just for illustration), then $a = 3$.

This invariance is extremely useful for checking your work when converting between forms. If the $a$ value changes, you have made an error.

4.4 — Transformations of the Parent Function

Every quadratic function can be viewed as a transformation of the parent function $f(x) = x^2$. The general transformation equation is:

$$f(x) = a(x - h)^2 + k$$

where: - $a$ controls vertical stretch/compression and reflection - $h$ controls horizontal translation - $k$ controls vertical translation

4.4.1 — Horizontal Translation: The Role of $h$

The term $(x - h)$ shifts the graph horizontally:

  • $h > 0$: Shift right by $h$ units. Example: $f(x) = (x - 4)^2$ shifts the vertex from $(0, 0)$ to $(4, 0)$.
  • $h < 0$: Shift left by $|h|$ units. Example: $f(x) = (x + 2)^2 = (x - (-2))^2$ shifts the vertex from $(0, 0)$ to $(-2, 0)$.

Why the sign is counterintuitive: The expression $(x - h)$ equals zero when $x = h$. Since the vertex of the parent function occurs where the squared term equals zero, the new vertex occurs at $x = h$. When we write $(x + 2)$, this equals zero when $x = -2$, so the vertex shifts to $x = -2$.

4.4.2 — Vertical Translation: The Role of $k$

The term $+ k$ shifts the graph vertically:

  • $k > 0$: Shift up by $k$ units. Example: $f(x) = x^2 + 5$ shifts the vertex from $(0, 0)$ to $(0, 5)$.
  • $k < 0$: Shift down by $|k|$ units. Example: $f(x) = x^2 - 3$ shifts the vertex from $(0, 0)$ to $(0, -3)$.

4.4.3 — Combined Transformations

When multiple transformations are applied, the order of operations determines the final position of the vertex:

  1. Start with the parent function $f(x) = x^2$ with vertex at $(0, 0)$.
  2. Apply the horizontal shift $h$: vertex moves to $(h, 0)$.
  3. Apply the vertical stretch/compression and reflection $a$: vertex stays at $(h, 0)$ but the shape changes.
  4. Apply the vertical shift $k$: vertex moves to $(h, k)$.

Example: Describe the transformations of $f(x) = -2(x + 3)^2 - 4$.

Rewriting: $f(x) = -2(x - (-3))^2 + (-4)$

  • $a = -2$: Vertical stretch by factor of 2, reflection across x-axis (opens downward)
  • $h = -3$: Shift left by 3 units
  • $k = -4$: Shift down by 4 units
  • Vertex: $(-3, -4)$

4.4.4 — Transformation Order and the SAT

The SAT may describe transformations in words and ask you to identify the resulting equation, or vice versa. Key phrases to recognize:

Phrase Mathematical Effect
"Shifted 3 units to the right" Replace $x$ with $(x - 3)$, so $h = 3$
"Shifted 2 units to the left" Replace $x$ with $(x + 2)$, so $h = -2$
"Shifted 5 units up" Add 5 to the function, so $k = 5$
"Shifted 4 units down" Subtract 4 from the function, so $k = -4$
"Reflected across the x-axis" Multiply the function by $-1$, so $a$ becomes negative
"Vertically stretched by a factor of 3" Multiply the function by 3, so $a = 3$
"Vertically compressed by a factor of 1/2" Multiply the function by 1/2, so $a = 1/2$

4.5 — The Axis of Symmetry

The axis of symmetry is the vertical line that passes through the vertex and divides the parabola into two congruent halves. Every point on the parabola has a mirror image across this line.

Finding the Axis of Symmetry

From any form of the quadratic:

  • Standard Form: $x = -\frac{b}{2a}$
  • Vertex Form: $x = h$
  • Intercept Form: $x = \frac{p + q}{2}$

All three formulas give the same result because they all describe the same parabola.

Properties of the Axis of Symmetry

  1. It is always a vertical line (equation of the form $x = \text{constant}$).
  2. It passes through the vertex.
  3. The function values at equal distances on either side of the axis of symmetry are equal. That is, $f(h + d) = f(h - d)$ for any value $d$.
  4. The axis of symmetry is the perpendicular bisector of any horizontal segment connecting two points on the parabola at the same height.

Using the Axis of Symmetry

If you know one point on the parabola and the axis of symmetry, you can find its mirror image. For example, if the axis of symmetry is $x = 3$ and the parabola passes through $(7, 10)$, then it also passes through $(-1, 10)$ because $7 - 3 = 4$ and $3 - 4 = -1$.

4.6 — Roots, Zeros, and x-Intercepts

The terms roots, zeros, and x-intercepts are often used interchangeably on the SAT, though they have slightly different connotations:

  • x-intercepts: The point(s) where the graph crosses the x-axis. Written as coordinate pairs $(p, 0)$.
  • Zeros: The x-values that make $f(x) = 0$. These are the x-coordinates of the x-intercepts.
  • Roots: The solutions to the equation $f(x) = 0$. Same as zeros.

Finding Roots by Setting $f(x) = 0$

To find the roots of any quadratic, set the function equal to zero and solve for $x$. The method depends on the form:

From Intercept Form: $a(x - p)(x - q) = 0$

By the Zero Product Property: $x = p$ or $x = q$.

From Vertex Form: $a(x - h)^2 + k = 0$

Isolate the squared term: $(x - h)^2 = -\frac{k}{a}$

Take the square root: $x - h = \pm\sqrt{-\frac{k}{a}}$

Solve: $x = h \pm \sqrt{-\frac{k}{a}}$

From Standard Form: $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$

Use the quadratic formula (covered in Section 4.8).

The Three Possible Root Scenarios

A quadratic function can have exactly 0, 1, or 2 real roots. This is determined by the discriminant (covered in Section 4.7).

  1. Two distinct real roots: The parabola crosses the x-axis at two points.
  2. One real root (double root): The parabola touches the x-axis at exactly one point (the vertex lies on the x-axis).
  3. No real roots: The parabola never intersects the x-axis (it floats entirely above or below it).

The y-Intercept

While not a root, the y-intercept is another critical point. It is found by evaluating $f(0)$:

  • Standard Form: $f(0) = c$, so the y-intercept is $(0, c)$.
  • Vertex Form: $f(0) = a(0 - h)^2 + k = ah^2 + k$, so the y-intercept is $(0, ah^2 + k)$.
  • Intercept Form: $f(0) = a(0 - p)(0 - q) = apq$, so the y-intercept is $(0, apq)$.

4.7 — The Discriminant: A Complete Analysis

The discriminant is the expression under the radical in the quadratic formula. For a quadratic in standard form $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$, the discriminant is:

$$\Delta = b^2 - 4ac$$

The discriminant is one of the most powerful tools in quadratic analysis because it tells you exactly how many real roots the quadratic has without requiring you to actually solve for them.

4.7.1 — The Three Cases

Case 1: $\Delta > 0$ (Positive Discriminant)

  • The quadratic has two distinct real roots.
  • The parabola crosses the x-axis at two different points.
  • If $\Delta$ is a perfect square, the roots are rational numbers (and the quadratic can be factored over the integers).
  • If $\Delta$ is not a perfect square, the roots are irrational numbers (involving radicals).

Case 2: $\Delta = 0$ (Zero Discriminant)

  • The quadratic has exactly one real root (also called a double root or repeated root).
  • The parabola touches the x-axis at exactly one point — the vertex lies on the x-axis.
  • The single root is $x = -\frac{b}{2a}$.
  • The quadratic can be written as a perfect square: $a(x - r)^2$ where $r$ is the repeated root.

Case 3: $\Delta < 0$ (Negative Discriminant)

  • The quadratic has no real roots (it has two complex conjugate roots).
  • The parabola never intersects the x-axis.
  • The parabola lies entirely above the x-axis (if $a > 0$) or entirely below it (if $a < 0$).

4.7.2 — Graphical Interpretation of the Discriminant

The discriminant tells you where the parabola sits relative to the x-axis:

Discriminant Number of x-intercepts Vertex Position Graph
$\Delta > 0$ 2 Above or below x-axis Crosses x-axis twice
$\Delta = 0$ 1 On the x-axis Touches x-axis at vertex
$\Delta < 0$ 0 Above (if $a>0$) or below (if $a<0$) x-axis Never touches x-axis

4.7.3 — Computing the Discriminant: Step by Step

Step 1: Ensure the equation is in standard form $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$.

Step 2: Identify $a$, $b$, and $c$ with their signs.

Step 3: Substitute into $\Delta = b^2 - 4ac$.

Step 4: Evaluate and interpret.

Example 1: Find the discriminant of $2x^2 - 5x + 3 = 0$.

$a = 2$, $b = -5$, $c = 3$

$\Delta = (-5)^2 - 4(2)(3) = 25 - 24 = 1$

Since $\Delta = 1 > 0$ and 1 is a perfect square, there are two distinct rational roots.

Example 2: Find the discriminant of $x^2 - 6x + 9 = 0$.

$a = 1$, $b = -6$, $c = 9$

$\Delta = (-6)^2 - 4(1)(9) = 36 - 36 = 0$

Since $\Delta = 0$, there is exactly one real root (a double root). Indeed, $x^2 - 6x + 9 = (x - 3)^2$, so the double root is $x = 3$.

Example 3: Find the discriminant of $3x^2 + 2x + 5 = 0$.

$a = 3$, $b = 2$, $c = 5$

$\Delta = (2)^2 - 4(3)(5) = 4 - 60 = -56$

Since $\Delta = -56 < 0$, there are no real roots.

4.7.4 — Common Discriminant Traps on the SAT

Trap 1: Forgetting the sign of $b$. If $b$ is negative, $b^2$ is still positive. For $x^2 - 4x + 4 = 0$, $b = -4$, so $b^2 = (-4)^2 = 16$, NOT $-16$.

Trap 2: Forgetting to multiply by 4. The discriminant is $b^2 - 4ac$, not $b^2 - ac$. The factor of 4 is essential.

Trap 3: Misidentifying $a$, $b$, and $c$. The equation must be in standard form with zero on one side. For $2x^2 = 5x - 3$, first rewrite as $2x^2 - 5x + 3 = 0$. Then $a = 2$, $b = -5$, $c = 3$.

Trap 4: Confusing the discriminant with the quadratic formula. The discriminant is just $b^2 - 4ac$, not the full formula $\frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}$.

4.7.5 — The Discriminant and the Vertex

The discriminant also tells us about the relationship between the vertex and the x-axis:

  • If $\Delta > 0$ and $a > 0$: The vertex is below the x-axis (minimum point is negative).
  • If $\Delta > 0$ and $a < 0$: The vertex is above the x-axis (maximum point is positive).
  • If $\Delta = 0$: The vertex is on the x-axis.
  • If $\Delta < 0$ and $a > 0$: The vertex is above the x-axis (minimum point is positive).
  • If $\Delta < 0$ and $a < 0$: The vertex is below the x-axis (maximum point is negative).

4.8 — The Quadratic Formula

The quadratic formula provides a universal method for finding the roots of any quadratic equation in standard form:

$$x = \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}$$

This formula works for every quadratic, regardless of whether it can be factored.

4.8.1 — Derivation Overview (Completing the Square)

The quadratic formula is derived by applying the method of completing the square to the general quadratic equation $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$:

  1. Divide by $a$: $x^2 + \frac{b}{a}x + \frac{c}{a} = 0$
  2. Move the constant: $x^2 + \frac{b}{a}x = -\frac{c}{a}$
  3. Complete the square: $x^2 + \frac{b}{a}x + \frac{b^2}{4a^2} = \frac{b^2}{4a^2} - \frac{c}{a}$
  4. Factor the left side: $\left(x + \frac{b}{2a}\right)^2 = \frac{b^2 - 4ac}{4a^2}$
  5. Take the square root: $x + \frac{b}{2a} = \pm\frac{\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}$
  6. Solve for $x$: $x = -\frac{b}{2a} \pm \frac{\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a} = \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}$

4.8.2 — Using the Quadratic Formula

Step 1: Write the equation in standard form $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$.

Step 2: Identify $a$, $b$, and $c$ with their signs.

Step 3: Substitute into the formula.

Step 4: Simplify the discriminant first.

Step 5: Simplify the entire expression.

Example: Solve $3x^2 - 7x + 2 = 0$.

$a = 3$, $b = -7$, $c = 2$

$$x = \frac{-(-7) \pm \sqrt{(-7)^2 - 4(3)(2)}}{2(3)}$$

$$x = \frac{7 \pm \sqrt{49 - 24}}{6}$$

$$x = \frac{7 \pm \sqrt{25}}{6}$$

$$x = \frac{7 \pm 5}{6}$$

$$x = \frac{7 + 5}{6} = \frac{12}{6} = 2 \quad \text{or} \quad x = \frac{7 - 5}{6} = \frac{2}{6} = \frac{1}{3}$$

The roots are $x = 2$ and $x = \frac{1}{3}$.

4.8.3 — The Quadratic Formula and the Discriminant

The discriminant appears directly inside the quadratic formula as the expression under the radical. This is why the discriminant determines the nature of the roots:

  • If $b^2 - 4ac > 0$: The $\pm$ produces two different real values.
  • If $b^2 - 4ac = 0$: The $\pm$ produces the same value twice (since $\sqrt{0} = 0$).
  • If $b^2 - 4ac < 0$: The square root of a negative number is not real, so there are no real solutions.

4.8.4 — Simplifying Radical Answers

On the SAT, answers involving radicals must be fully simplified:

  1. Factor out perfect squares from under the radical.
  2. Reduce any common factors between the numerator and denominator.

Example: $x = \frac{6 \pm \sqrt{48}}{4}$

Simplify $\sqrt{48} = \sqrt{16 \cdot 3} = 4\sqrt{3}$

$x = \frac{6 \pm 4\sqrt{3}}{4} = \frac{2(3 \pm 2\sqrt{3})}{4} = \frac{3 \pm 2\sqrt{3}}{2}$

4.9 — Sum and Product of Roots

For a quadratic equation $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$ with roots $r_1$ and $r_2$, the following relationships hold:

4.9.1 — Sum of Roots

$$r_1 + r_2 = -\frac{b}{a}$$

4.9.2 — Product of Roots

$$r_1 \cdot r_2 = \frac{c}{a}$$

4.9.3 — Derivation from Factored Form

If the roots are $r_1$ and $r_2$, the quadratic can be written as:

$$a(x - r_1)(x - r_2) = 0$$

Expanding:

$$a(x^2 - r_1x - r_2x + r_1r_2) = 0$$

$$ax^2 - a(r_1 + r_2)x + ar_1r_2 = 0$$

Comparing with $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$:

  • Coefficient of $x$: $-a(r_1 + r_2) = b \Rightarrow r_1 + r_2 = -\frac{b}{a}$
  • Constant term: $ar_1r_2 = c \Rightarrow r_1r_2 = \frac{c}{a}$

4.9.4 — Applications on the SAT

Finding the quadratic given the roots: If the roots are $x = 3$ and $x = -5$, then: - Sum of roots: $3 + (-5) = -2 = -\frac{b}{a}$ - Product of roots: $3 \cdot (-5) = -15 = \frac{c}{a}$

If $a = 1$, then $b = 2$ and $c = -15$, giving us $x^2 + 2x - 15 = 0$.

Finding the vertex x-coordinate: Since the axis of symmetry lies halfway between the roots:

$$x_{\text{vertex}} = \frac{r_1 + r_2}{2} = \frac{-b/a}{2} = -\frac{b}{2a}$$

This confirms the vertex formula from the sum of roots.

Finding a missing root: If one root of $2x^2 - 10x + c = 0$ is $x = 3$, find the other root.

Using the sum of roots: $r_1 + r_2 = -\frac{-10}{2} = 5$

So $3 + r_2 = 5$, giving $r_2 = 2$.

4.9.5 — Vieta's Formulas (Extended Context)

The sum and product of roots formulas are sometimes called Vieta's formulas. While the SAT doesn't use this name, the concept appears frequently. For higher-degree polynomials, Vieta's formulas extend to relationships between all roots and coefficients, but for quadratics, only the sum and product are relevant.

4.10 — Completing the Square

Completing the square is the process of converting a quadratic from Standard Form to Vertex Form. It is the method by which the vertex form is derived and is essential for finding the vertex of a quadratic that doesn't factor nicely.

4.10.1 — The Method: When $a = 1$

Goal: Convert $x^2 + bx + c$ to $(x + d)^2 + e$ form.

Step 1: Start with $x^2 + bx + c$.

Step 2: Take half of $b$: $\frac{b}{2}$.

Step 3: Square it: $\left(\frac{b}{2}\right)^2 = \frac{b^2}{4}$.

Step 4: Add and subtract this value:

$$x^2 + bx + \frac{b^2}{4} - \frac{b^2}{4} + c$$

Step 5: Group the perfect square trinomial:

$$\left(x + \frac{b}{2}\right)^2 - \frac{b^2}{4} + c$$

Step 6: Simplify the constant term.

Example: Convert $x^2 - 8x + 5$ to vertex form.

  • $b = -8$, so $\frac{b}{2} = -4$ and $\left(\frac{b}{2}\right)^2 = 16$
  • $x^2 - 8x + 16 - 16 + 5$
  • $(x - 4)^2 - 11$

The vertex is $(4, -11)$.

4.10.2 — The Method: When $a \neq 1$

Goal: Convert $ax^2 + bx + c$ to $a(x - h)^2 + k$ form.

Step 1: Factor $a$ from the $x^2$ and $x$ terms only:

$$a\left(x^2 + \frac{b}{a}x\right) + c$$

Step 2: Inside the parentheses, take half of $\frac{b}{a}$: $\frac{b}{2a}$.

Step 3: Square it: $\left(\frac{b}{2a}\right)^2 = \frac{b^2}{4a^2}$.

Step 4: Add and subtract this value inside the parentheses:

$$a\left(x^2 + \frac{b}{a}x + \frac{b^2}{4a^2} - \frac{b^2}{4a^2}\right) + c$$

Step 5: Group the perfect square trinomial:

$$a\left(\left(x + \frac{b}{2a}\right)^2 - \frac{b^2}{4a^2}\right) + c$$

Step 6: Distribute $a$:

$$a\left(x + \frac{b}{2a}\right)^2 - \frac{b^2}{4a} + c$$

Step 7: Simplify the constant term:

$$a\left(x + \frac{b}{2a}\right)^2 + \left(c - \frac{b^2}{4a}\right)$$

Example: Convert $2x^2 - 12x + 11$ to vertex form.

  • Factor 2 from the first two terms: $2(x^2 - 6x) + 11$
  • Half of $-6$ is $-3$, squared is $9$
  • $2(x^2 - 6x + 9 - 9) + 11$
  • $2((x - 3)^2 - 9) + 11$
  • $2(x - 3)^2 - 18 + 11$
  • $2(x - 3)^2 - 7$

The vertex is $(3, -7)$.

4.10.3 — Why Completing the Square Works

The method works because we are creating a perfect square trinomial — a trinomial that can be expressed as the square of a binomial. The pattern is:

$$x^2 + 2dx + d^2 = (x + d)^2$$

By adding and subtracting $d^2$ (where $d = \frac{b}{2}$), we create this perfect square without changing the value of the expression.

4.10.4 — Completing the Square and the Quadratic Formula

As shown in Section 4.8.1, the quadratic formula itself is derived by completing the square on the general equation $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$. This demonstrates the deep connection between these two tools.

4.11 — The Zero Product Property

The Zero Product Property states:

$$\text{If } A \cdot B = 0, \text{ then } A = 0 \text{ or } B = 0 \text{ (or both).}$$

This property is the foundation for solving quadratic equations in factored form.

Application

Given $(x - 3)(x + 5) = 0$:

  • Either $x - 3 = 0 \Rightarrow x = 3$
  • Or $x + 5 = 0 \Rightarrow x = -5$

The solutions are $x = 3$ and $x = -5$.

Important Caveat

The Zero Product Property only works when the product equals zero. If $(x - 3)(x + 5) = 7$, you cannot set each factor equal to 7. You must first expand and rearrange to get zero on one side, then factor (if possible) and apply the property.

Higher Degree Applications

The Zero Product Property extends to any number of factors. If $(x - a)(x - b)(x - c) = 0$, then $x = a$, $x = b$, or $x = c$. This is why factoring is so powerful — it reduces a complex equation to a series of simple linear equations.

4.12 — Converting Between All Three Forms

The SAT frequently requires you to move fluidly between the three forms. Here is a complete guide:

4.12.1 — Standard Form → Vertex Form (Completing the Square)

Method: Complete the square as described in Section 4.10.

Example: $x^2 - 6x + 2$

  • Half of $-6$ is $-3$, squared is $9$
  • $(x^2 - 6x + 9) - 9 + 2$
  • $(x - 3)^2 - 7$

4.12.2 — Standard Form → Intercept Form (Factoring)

Method 1: Factoring. If the quadratic factors nicely, factor it directly.

Example: $x^2 - 5x + 6 = (x - 2)(x - 3)$

Method 2: Quadratic Formula. If the quadratic doesn't factor easily, use the quadratic formula to find the roots $r_1$ and $r_2$, then write $a(x - r_1)(x - r_2)$.

Example: $2x^2 - 5x - 3$

Using the quadratic formula: $x = \frac{5 \pm \sqrt{25 + 24}}{4} = \frac{5 \pm 7}{4}$

$x = 3$ or $x = -\frac{1}{2}$

So $2(x - 3)\left(x + \frac{1}{2}\right)$ or equivalently $(x - 3)(2x + 1)$.

4.12.3 — Vertex Form → Standard Form (Expanding)

Method: Expand the squared term and distribute.

Example: $3(x - 2)^2 - 5$

  • $3(x^2 - 4x + 4) - 5$
  • $3x^2 - 12x + 12 - 5$
  • $3x^2 - 12x + 7$

4.12.4 — Intercept Form → Standard Form (Expanding)

Method: Multiply the factors using FOIL, then distribute $a$.

Example: $2(x - 1)(x + 4)$

  • $2(x^2 + 4x - x - 4)$
  • $2(x^2 + 3x - 4)$
  • $2x^2 + 6x - 8$

4.12.5 — Vertex Form → Intercept Form (and Vice Versa)

To convert between vertex and intercept forms, it is often easiest to go through standard form as an intermediate step:

Vertex → Intercept: Expand to standard form, then factor or use the quadratic formula.

Intercept → Vertex: Expand to standard form, then complete the square.

4.13 — Domain, Range, and End Behavior

4.13.1 — Domain

The domain of every quadratic function is all real numbers: $(-\infty, \infty)$ or $\mathbb{R}$. This is because you can substitute any real number for $x$ and obtain a real output.

4.13.2 — Range

The range depends on the direction of opening and the y-coordinate of the vertex:

  • If $a > 0$ (opens upward): Range is $[k, \infty)$ where $k$ is the y-coordinate of the vertex.
  • If $a < 0$ (opens downward): Range is $(-\infty, k]$ where $k$ is the y-coordinate of the vertex.

How to determine the range from each form:

Form Range (if $a > 0$) Range (if $a < 0$)
Standard $ax^2 + bx + c$ $\left[f!\left(-\frac{b}{2a}\right), \infty\right)$ $\left(-\infty, f!\left(-\frac{b}{2a}\right)\right]$
Vertex $a(x - h)^2 + k$ $[k, \infty)$ $(-\infty, k]$
Intercept $a(x - p)(x - q)$ $[f(\frac{p+q}{2}), \infty)$ $(-\infty, f(\frac{p+q}{2})]$

4.13.3 — End Behavior

End behavior describes what happens to $f(x)$ as $x$ approaches positive or negative infinity:

  • If $a > 0$: As $x \to \infty$, $f(x) \to \infty$ (rises to the right). As $x \to -\infty$, $f(x) \to \infty$ (rises to the left).
  • If $a < 0$: As $x \to \infty$, $f(x) \to -\infty$ (falls to the right). As $x \to -\infty$, $f(x) \to -\infty$ (falls to the left).

Both ends of the parabola go in the same direction (both up or both down), which distinguishes quadratics from cubics and other odd-degree polynomials.

4.13.4 — Intervals of Increase and Decrease

A parabola is not monotonic — it changes direction at the vertex:

  • If $a > 0$: Decreasing on $(-\infty, h)$ and increasing on $(h, \infty)$.
  • If $a < 0$: Increasing on $(-\infty, h)$ and decreasing on $(h, \infty)$.

The vertex $x = h$ is the point where the function transitions from increasing to decreasing (or vice versa).

4.14 — Quadratic Modeling and Real-World Applications

The SAT frequently presents quadratics in the context of real-world scenarios. Common applications include:

4.14.1 — Projectile Motion

Objects launched or thrown near Earth's surface follow parabolic trajectories (ignoring air resistance). The height $h$ as a function of time $t$ is:

$$h(t) = -\frac{g}{2}t^2 + v_0t + h_0$$

where $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, $v_0$ is the initial velocity, and $h_0$ is the initial height.

On the SAT, this is often simplified to something like $h(t) = -16t^2 + 64t + 80$ (using feet and seconds).

Key interpretations: - The negative leading coefficient reflects that gravity pulls the object downward. - The vertex represents the maximum height and the time at which it occurs. - The positive root (if it exists) represents when the object hits the ground. - The y-intercept represents the initial height.

4.14.2 — Area Problems

Quadratic functions naturally arise in area problems where one dimension depends on the other. For example, if a rectangle has a fixed perimeter of 40 units and one side has length $x$, the other side has length $20 - x$, and the area is:

$$A(x) = x(20 - x) = 20x - x^2 = -x^2 + 20x$$

The maximum area occurs at the vertex: $x = -\frac{20}{2(-1)} = 10$, giving a square with area 100 square units.

4.14.3 — Profit and Revenue Optimization

Business applications often use quadratics to model profit or revenue as a function of price or quantity. The vertex then represents the optimal price or quantity that maximizes profit.

4.14.4 — Interpreting the Vertex in Context

When a quadratic models a real-world scenario, the vertex has a specific meaning:

  • In projectile motion: maximum height
  • In area problems: maximum area
  • In profit problems: maximum profit
  • In cost problems: minimum cost

The SAT may ask you to interpret the vertex in context, requiring you to identify both the numerical value and its real-world meaning.

4.14.5 — Interpreting the Roots in Context

The roots of a quadratic in a modeling context often represent:

  • Break-even points (where profit = 0)
  • Impact times (when a projectile hits the ground)
  • Boundary values (where a dimension becomes zero)

If one root is negative and the other is positive, the negative root is often extraneous in the real-world context (you can't have negative time or negative length).

4.15 — Advanced Quadratic Concepts for the SAT

4.15.1 — The Vertex as the Point of Extremum

The vertex is the only point where the derivative (rate of change) of the quadratic is zero. While calculus is not tested on the SAT, the concept that the vertex represents the "turning point" is essential. At the vertex:

  • The function stops increasing and starts decreasing (or vice versa).
  • The function achieves its absolute maximum or absolute minimum value.

4.15.2 — Symmetry and Equivalent Points

Because of the parabola's symmetry, if $f(h + d) = f(h - d)$ for any $d$, then the points $(h + d, f(h + d))$ and $(h - d, f(h - d))$ are symmetric about the axis of symmetry.

This property is useful when the SAT gives you one point and asks you to find another point with the same y-value.

4.15.3 — The Relationship Between the Discriminant and the Vertex Height

The discriminant and the vertex are intimately connected. The y-coordinate of the vertex can be expressed as:

$$k = -\frac{\Delta}{4a} = -\frac{b^2 - 4ac}{4a} = \frac{4ac - b^2}{4a}$$

This formula reveals:

  • If $\Delta > 0$ and $a > 0$: $k < 0$ (vertex is below the x-axis)
  • If $\Delta = 0$: $k = 0$ (vertex is on the x-axis)
  • If $\Delta < 0$ and $a > 0$: $k > 0$ (vertex is above the x-axis)

4.15.4 — Quadratic Inequalities (Conceptual Understanding)

While the SAT rarely asks you to solve quadratic inequalities directly, understanding them helps with graphing and interpretation:

  • $ax^2 + bx + c > 0$: The region where the parabola is above the x-axis.
  • $ax^2 + bx + c < 0$: The region where the parabola is below the x-axis.

The solution depends on the direction of opening and the roots:

Case $ax^2 + bx + c > 0$ $ax^2 + bx + c < 0$
$a > 0$, two roots $p < q$ $x < p$ or $x > q$ $p < x < q$
$a < 0$, two roots $p < q$ $p < x < q$ $x < p$ or $x > q$
$a > 0$, no real roots All real numbers No solution
$a < 0$, no real roots No solution All real numbers

4.15.5 — Systems Involving Quadratics

The SAT may present systems where one equation is linear and the other is quadratic. These are solved by substitution:

  1. Solve the linear equation for one variable.
  2. Substitute into the quadratic equation.
  3. Solve the resulting quadratic.
  4. Find the other variable for each solution.

The number of intersection points (0, 1, or 2) corresponds to the number of solutions to the system.

4.16 — Common SAT Traps and How to Avoid Them

4.16.1 — The Sign Trap in Vertex Form

The trap: In $f(x) = a(x - h)^2 + k$, the vertex is $(h, k)$. But when you see $(x + 3)^2$, the value of $h$ is $-3$, not $+3$.

How to avoid: Always rewrite the expression inside the parentheses as $(x - \text{something})$. If you see $(x + 3)$, rewrite it as $(x - (-3))$ to see that $h = -3$.

4.16.2 — The Sign Trap in Intercept Form

The trap: In $f(x) = a(x - p)(x - q)$, the x-intercepts are $p$ and $q$. But when you see $(x + 5)$, the intercept is $-5$, not $+5$.

How to avoid: Set each factor equal to zero and solve. $(x + 5) = 0$ gives $x = -5$.

4.16.3 — Confusing the Vertex with the Roots

The trap: The vertex and the roots are different things. The roots are where $f(x) = 0$. The vertex is the turning point. They coincide only when the discriminant is zero.

How to avoid: Remember that roots are x-values where the output is zero. The vertex is the input-output pair $(h, k)$ where the function achieves its extreme value.

4.16.4 — Forgetting the Negative Sign in $-\frac{b}{2a}$

The trap: The x-coordinate of the vertex is $-\frac{b}{2a}$, not $\frac{b}{2a}$.

How to avoid: The negative sign is part of the formula. Think of it as "the opposite of $b$, divided by $2a$."

4.16.5 — Assuming All Quadratics Can Be Factored

The trap: Not all quadratics factor nicely over the integers. Some have irrational roots, and some have no real roots.

How to avoid: Check the discriminant first. If it's not a perfect square, the roots are irrational. If it's negative, there are no real roots. Use the quadratic formula when factoring fails.

4.16.6 — Mishandling the Leading Coefficient

The trap: When completing the square with $a \neq 1$, students forget to factor $a$ from the first two terms, or they forget to multiply the subtracted term by $a$ when removing it from the parentheses.

How to avoid: Be systematic. Factor $a$ from the $x$ terms, complete the square inside the parentheses, then distribute $a$ to the term being removed.

4.16.7 — Confusing the Discriminant with the Quadratic Formula

The trap: The discriminant is just $b^2 - 4ac$, not the entire quadratic formula.

How to avoid: Remember that the discriminant is the expression under the radical in the quadratic formula. It tells you about the nature of the roots, but it is not the roots themselves.

4.16.8 — Not Simplifying Completely

The trap: Leaving answers in unsimplified form, such as $\frac{6 \pm \sqrt{48}}{4}$ instead of $\frac{3 \pm 2\sqrt{3}}{2}$.

How to avoid: Always check if the radical can be simplified (factor out perfect squares) and if the fraction can be reduced.

4.17 — Strategic Approaches for SAT Quadratic Problems

4.17.1 — Using the Desmos Calculator

The Digital SAT includes the Desmos graphing calculator, which is extraordinarily powerful for quadratic problems:

  • Finding roots: Graph the function and click on the x-intercepts.
  • Finding the vertex: Graph the function and click on the turning point.
  • Solving systems: Graph both equations and find the intersection points.
  • Checking your work: Graph your answer to verify it matches the problem conditions.

When to use Desmos: - When the quadratic is messy or doesn't factor easily. - When you need to find decimal approximations. - When you want to verify your algebraic solution.

4.17.2 — Backsolving

When the answer choices are specific numbers, you can substitute them into the original equation to see which one works. This is particularly useful for:

  • Finding roots when the choices are x-values.
  • Finding coefficients when the choices are parameter values.

4.17.3 — Process of Elimination

Use the properties of quadratics to eliminate impossible answers:

  • If the parabola opens upward, eliminate answers that suggest a maximum.
  • If the discriminant is negative, eliminate answers that suggest real roots.
  • If the vertex is at $(2, -3)$, eliminate answers that suggest a different vertex.

4.17.4 — Identifying the Form That Reveals the Answer

Before solving, identify which form the quadratic is in and what information is immediately available:

  • Standard Form: y-intercept is $c$, use $-\frac{b}{2a}$ for vertex.
  • Vertex Form: vertex is $(h, k)$, axis of symmetry is $x = h$.
  • Intercept Form: x-intercepts are $p$ and $q$, vertex x-coordinate is $\frac{p+q}{2}$.

Choose the approach that requires the least computation.

4.18 — Comprehensive Decision Framework

When encountering a quadratic problem on the SAT, follow this decision framework:

Step 1: Identify the Given Form

  • Is the quadratic in Standard, Vertex, or Intercept form?
  • What information is immediately visible?

Step 2: Identify What Is Being Asked

  • Are you looking for the vertex? The roots? The y-intercept? The discriminant? The equation itself?

Step 3: Choose the Optimal Strategy

If you need... And the form is... Then use...
Vertex Standard $x = -\frac{b}{2a}$
Vertex Vertex Read $(h, k)$ directly
Vertex Intercept $x = \frac{p+q}{2}$
Roots Standard Quadratic formula or factoring
Roots Vertex Solve $a(x-h)^2 + k = 0$
Roots Intercept Read $p$ and $q$ directly
y-intercept Standard $c$
y-intercept Vertex Evaluate $f(0) = ah^2 + k$
y-intercept Intercept Evaluate $f(0) = apq$
Discriminant Standard $b^2 - 4ac$
Discriminant Vertex Convert to standard first
Discriminant Intercept Convert to standard first

Step 4: Verify Your Answer

  • Check that the signs are correct.
  • Check that the form matches what the question asks for.
  • If possible, verify using Desmos or by substituting back.

4.19 — The Interconnected Nature of Quadratic Concepts

One of the most important insights for mastering quadratics is understanding how all the concepts connect:

  1. The three forms are equivalent representations of the same function. Converting between them is a mechanical process (completing the square, expanding, factoring).

  2. The discriminant determines the nature of the roots, which determines how the graph intersects the x-axis, which determines the sign of the vertex's y-coordinate.

  3. The vertex is the midpoint of the roots (when they exist), and its x-coordinate is the average of the roots, which equals $-\frac{b}{2a}$.

  4. The leading coefficient $a$ is invariant across all three forms and controls both the direction of opening and the "width" of the parabola.

  5. The sum and product of roots provide a shortcut to reconstructing the quadratic from its roots and offer a check on your work.

Understanding these connections allows you to approach any quadratic problem from multiple angles and verify your answers through cross-checking.

4.20 — Final Notes on Quadratic Mastery

Mastering quadratics for the SAT requires:
