Percent of $\frac{\%}{100} \times \text{whole}$
Percent change $\frac{\text{new} - \text{old}}{\text{old}} \times 100\%$
Multiplier (increase) $1 + r$
Multiplier (decrease) $1 - r$
Reverse percent Divide by the multiplier
Successive changes Multiply the individual multipliers
Linear scaling Factor $k$
Area scaling Factor $k^2$
Volume scaling Factor $k^3$
Linear from area $k = \sqrt{\text{area factor}}$
Linear from volume $k = \sqrt[3]{\text{volume factor}}$
Mixture equation $A_1c_1 + A_2c_2 = (A_1 + A_2)c_t$
Distance-Rate-Time $d = rt$
Simple interest $I = Prt$
Compound interest $A = P(1 + \frac{r}{n})^{nt}$

This chapter has provided an exhaustive treatment of ratios, rates, proportions, percent change, and scaling laws. Mastery of these interconnected concepts — and the ability to fluidly move between them — is essential for SAT Math success. The multiplicative mindset developed here will serve as the foundation for advanced topics in algebra, geometry, data analysis, and real-world mathematical modeling.

Chapter 7: Comprehensive Geometry — Lines, Angles, Triangles, Polygons, and Solid Figures

7.0 — Introduction: The Role of Geometry on the SAT

Geometry occupies a unique position on the SAT Math section. Accounting for approximately 15% of total math questions — roughly 5 to 7 questions out of 44 — it is one of the smaller content domains. However, its questions tend to be highly conceptual and are designed to exploit common misconceptions acquired during standard geometry instruction.

This chapter provides a rigorous, exhaustive treatment of every geometric concept the SAT tests. The emphasis is on precision: precise definitions, precise theorems, precise formulas, and precise strategies for avoiding the traps the SAT sets.

Important Note: Most geometric figures on the SAT are drawn to scale unless explicitly labeled otherwise. This means diagrams can be used for estimation and verification, but visual estimation alone is never a substitute for analytical reasoning.

7.1 — Lines and Angles: The Foundation

7.1.1 — Fundamental Definitions

  • Point: A location in space with no dimension (no length, width, or height). Represented by a dot and labeled with a capital letter, such as point $A$.
  • Line: A set of points extending infinitely in two opposite directions. Has no endpoints and no thickness. Denoted as $\overleftrightarrow{AB}$ (the line passing through points $A$ and $B$) or by a lowercase script letter such as $l$.
  • Ray: A part of a line that has one endpoint and extends infinitely in one direction. Denoted as $\overrightarrow{AB}$ (endpoint $A$, extending through $B$).
  • Line Segment: A part of a line bounded by two distinct endpoints. Denoted as $\overline{AB}$, where $A$ and $B$ are the endpoints.
  • Collinear Points: Points that lie on the same line.
  • Coplanar Points: Points that lie in the same plane.

A line extends infinitely in both directions and has no measurable length. A line segment has a definite, measurable length, computed via the distance formula.

7.1.2 — Angle Formation and Measurement

An angle is the figure formed by two rays sharing a common endpoint. The common endpoint is the vertex, and the two rays are the sides of the angle.

Angle measure denotes the amount of rotation between the two sides — the degree to which one ray must be rotated about the vertex to coincide with the other. Angles are measured in degrees ($^\circ$), where a complete rotation (a full circle) constitutes $360^\circ$.

  • A straight angle measures $180^\circ$ (the rays point in opposite directions).
  • A right angle measures $90^\circ$ (half of a straight angle).

7.1.3 — Classification of Angles

Classification Measure Description
Acute Angle $0^\circ < \theta < 90^\circ$ Strictly between zero and ninety degrees
Right Angle $\theta = 90^\circ$ Exactly ninety degrees; denoted by a small square at the vertex
Obtuse Angle $90^\circ < \theta < 180^\circ$ Strictly between ninety and 180 degrees
Straight Angle $\theta = 180^\circ$ Exactly 180 degrees; forms a straight line
Reflex Angle $180^\circ < \theta < 360^\circ$ Greater than $180^\circ$ but less than a full circle

7.1.4 — Angle Relationships

Supplementary Angles

Two angles whose measures sum to $180^\circ$ are supplementary. When two angles form a linear pair (adjacent angles whose non-common sides form a straight line), they are supplementary.

If two angles are supplementary and one measures $(3x + 10)^\circ$ while the other measures $(2x - 5)^\circ$, then:

$$(3x + 10) + (2x - 5) = 180$$ $$5x + 5 = 180$$ $$x = 35$$

The angles measure $115^\circ$ and $65^\circ$.

Complementary Angles

Two angles whose measures sum to $90^\circ$ are complementary. The two acute angles of a right triangle are always complementary because the triangle's interior angle sum is $180^\circ$ and the right angle accounts for $90^\circ$.

The complementary angle to $35^\circ$ is $90^\circ - 35^\circ = 55^\circ$.

Vertical Angles

When two lines intersect, they form four angles. The are vertical angles (or vertically opposite angles), and they are always congruent (equal in measure).

If two intersecting lines create angles of $x^\circ$, $(180 - x)^\circ$, $x^\circ$, and $(180 - x)^\circ$, the opposite pairs are congruent.

Angles Formed by a Transversal Intersecting Parallel Lines

When a third line (the transversal) cuts through two parallel lines, it creates eight angles with specific relationships:

Corresponding Angles: Angles in the same relative position at each intersection. They are congruent.

Alternate Interior Angles: Angles between the two parallel lines, on opposite sides of the transversal. They are congruent.

Alternate Exterior Angles: Angles outside the two parallel lines, on opposite sides of the transversal. They are congruent.

Consecutive Interior Angles (Same-Side Interior): Angles between the two parallel lines, on the same side of the transversal. They are supplementary (sum to $180^\circ$).

Summary: Corresponding = equal. Alternate = equal. Same-side = supplementary.

Once the measure of any single angle in this configuration is known, the measures of all seven other angles can be determined through these relationships.

7.1.5 — Perpendicular Lines

Two lines are perpendicular if they intersect at a right angle ($90^\circ$). The symbol for perpendicularity is $\perp$.

In coordinate geometry, two non-vertical, non-horizontal lines are perpendicular if and only if their slopes are negative reciprocals of each other. If one line has slope $m$, the perpendicular line has slope $-\frac{1}{m}$.

A horizontal line (slope $0$) is perpendicular to a vertical line (undefined slope).

7.1.6 — Angle Bisectors

An angle bisector is a ray that divides an angle into two congruent angles. If ray $\overrightarrow{BD}$ bisects $\angle ABC$, then $\angle ABD \cong \angle DBC$, and each measures exactly half of $\angle ABC$.

7.2 — Triangles: The Fundamental Polygon

7.2.1 — The Triangle Angle Sum Theorem

The sum of the interior angles of any triangle is exactly $180^\circ$.

$$\angle A + \angle B + \angle C = 180^\circ$$

If two angles are known, the third is found by subtracting their sum from $180^\circ$.

7.2.2 — The Exterior Angle Theorem

An exterior angle of a triangle is formed by extending one side. The measure of an exterior angle equals the sum of the measures of the two remote interior angles (the two interior angles not adjacent to the exterior angle).

If a triangle has interior angles of $40^\circ$ and $65^\circ$, the exterior angle adjacent to the third interior angle equals $40^\circ + 65^\circ = 105^\circ$.

7.2.3 — The Triangle Inequality Theorem

The sum of the lengths of any two sides of a triangle must be strictly greater than the length of the third side.

For a triangle with side lengths $a$, $b$, and $c$:

$$a + b > c, \quad a + c > b, \quad b + c > a$$

If two sides have lengths $a$ and $b$ (with $a < b$), the third side $x$ satisfies:

$$b - a < x < a + b$$

Example: If two sides of a triangle are $5$ and $9$, the third side $x$ satisfies $4 < x < 14$.

7.2.4 — The Side-Angle Relationship Theorem

In any triangle, the longest side is opposite the largest angle, and the shortest side is opposite the smallest angle. This relationship is bidirectional: ranking side lengths determines the ranking of opposite angles, and vice versa.

7.2.5 — Classification of Triangles

By Sides: - Scalene: All three sides have different lengths. - Isosceles: Exactly two sides are congruent. - Equilateral: All three sides are congruent.

By Angles: - Acute Triangle: All three angles are acute. - Right Triangle: Exactly one angle is $90^\circ$. - Obtuse Triangle: Exactly one angle is obtuse.

7.2.6 — The Isosceles Triangle

Properties: - Two congruent sides (called the legs). - Two congruent angles (called the base angles), located opposite the legs. - The non-congruent side is called the base. - The altitude from the vertex angle to the base bisects both the vertex angle and the base.

SAT Strategy: In an isosceles triangle, once one base angle is known, the other base angle is identical (by congruence), and the vertex angle is $180^\circ - 2 \times (\text{base angle})$.

If the vertex angle is $x$ and each base angle is $y$, then $x + 2y = 180^\circ$.

7.2.7 — The Equilateral Triangle

Properties: - All three sides are congruent. - All three angles measure $60^\circ$. - Every altitude, median, angle bisector, and perpendicular bisector coincides. - The altitude divides the equilateral triangle into two $30^\circ\text{-}60^\circ\text{-}90^\circ$ right triangles.

Area Formula: For an equilateral triangle with side length $s$:

$$A = \frac{s^2\sqrt{3}}{4}$$

This formula is not provided on the SAT reference sheet and must be memorized. It derives from the general area formula $A = \frac{1}{2}bh$ where the height is $h = \frac{s\sqrt{3}}{2}$.

7.2.8 — The Right Triangle

Properties: - One angle measures exactly $90^\circ$. - The side opposite the right angle is the hypotenuse — always the longest side. - The other two sides are called legs. - The two acute angles are complementary.

7.2.9 — The Pythagorean Theorem

In a right triangle with legs of length $a$ and $b$ and hypotenuse of length $c$:

$$a^2 + b^2 = c^2$$

This theorem applies exclusively to right triangles. The converse is also true: if $a^2 + b^2 = c^2$ for three sides of a triangle, then the triangle is a right triangle with hypotenuse $c$.

Example: If legs are $3$ and $4$, the hypotenuse is $\sqrt{3^2 + 4^2} = \sqrt{25} = 5$.

7.2.10 — Pythagorean Triples

A Pythagorean Triple is a set of three positive integers $(a, b, c)$ satisfying $a^2 + b^2 = c^2$.

Essential Triples to Memorize:

Base Triple Common Multiples
$(3, 4, 5)$ $(6, 8, 10)$, $(9, 12, 15)$, $(12, 16, 20)$, $(15, 20, 25)$
$(5, 12, 13)$ $(10, 24, 26)$, $(15, 36, 39)$
$(8, 15, 17)$ $(16, 30, 34)$
$(7, 24, 25)$ $(14, 48, 50)$

Recognizing these triples instantly saves computation time on test day.

7.2.11 — Special Right Triangles

The $45^\circ\text{-}45^\circ\text{-}90^\circ$ Triangle

Formed by drawing a diagonal across a square. It is an isosceles right triangle.

Side Ratio: If each leg has length $s$, the hypotenuse has length $s\sqrt{2}$.

$$\text{leg} : \text{leg} : \text{hypotenuse} = s : s : s\sqrt{2}$$

Key Relationships: - Hypotenuse $= \text{leg} \times \sqrt{2}$ - $\text{Leg} = \frac{\text{hypotenuse}}{\sqrt{2}} = \frac{\text{hypotenuse} \cdot \sqrt{2}}{2}$

The diagonal of a square with side length $s$ is $s\sqrt{2}$.

The $30^\circ\text{-}60^\circ\text{-}90^\circ$ Triangle

Formed by drawing an altitude in an equilateral triangle.

Side Ratio: If the short leg (opposite the $30^\circ$ angle) has length $s$:

$$\text{short leg} : \text{long leg} : \text{hypotenuse} = s : s\sqrt{3} : 2s$$

The long leg is opposite the $60^\circ$ angle.

Key Relationships: - Hypotenuse $= 2 \times \text{short leg}$ - Short leg $= \frac{\text{hypotenuse}}{2}$ - Long leg $=$ \sqrt{3}$

7.3 — Area of Triangles

The area of a triangle is given by:

$$A = \frac{1}{2}bh$$

where $b$ is the base and $h$ is the height (altitude) perpendicular to that base.

Critical Note: The height is not necessarily one of the sides of the triangle. In an obtuse triangle, the height may fall outside the triangle. The height is always measured perpendicular to the chosen base.

Special Area Formulas:

  • Equilateral Triangle: $A = \frac{s^2\sqrt{3}}{4}$
  • Heron's Formula (for any triangle with sides $a$, $b$, $c$ and semiperimeter $s = \frac{a+b+c}{2}$): $$A = \sqrt{s(s-a)(s-b)(s-c)}$$
  • SAS Area Formula: If two sides and the included angle are known, $A = \frac{1}{2}ab\sin(C)$.

7.4 — Triangle Centers and Special Lines

  • Median: A segment from a vertex to the midpoint of the opposite side. intersect at the centroid, which divides each median in a $2:1$ ratio (the longer segment being closer to the vertex).
  • Altitude: A perpendicular segment from a vertex to the line containing the opposite side. The three altitudes intersect at the orthocenter.
  • Angle Bisector: A ray from a vertex that divides the angle into two congruent angles. The three angle bisectors intersect at the incenter (the center of the inscribed circle).
  • Perpendicular Bisector: A line perpendicular to a side at its midpoint. The three perpendicular bisectors intersect at the circumcenter (the center of the circumscribed circle).

7.5 — Similar Triangles

Similar triangles have the same shape but not necessarily the same size. Their corresponding angles are congruent, and their corresponding sides are proportional.

Notation: $\triangle ABC \sim \triangle DEF$ means triangle $ABC$ is similar to triangle $DEF$.

Properties: - Corresponding angles are equal. - Corresponding sides are proportional: $\frac{AB}{DE} = \frac{BC}{EF} = \frac{AC}{DF}$. - The ratio of their areas equals the square of the ratio of corresponding sides. - The ratio of their perimeters equals the ratio of corresponding sides.

Similarity Criteria:

  • AA (Angle-Angle): If two angles of one triangle are congruent to two angles of another, the triangles are similar.
  • SSS (Side-Side-Side): If all three corresponding sides are proportional, the triangles are similar.
  • SAS (Side-Angle-Side): If two corresponding sides are proportional and the included angle is congruent, the triangles are similar.

Common Similarity Configurations on the SAT:

  1. Parallel Line in a Triangle: If a line is parallel to one side of a triangle and intersects the other two sides, it creates a smaller triangle similar to the original.

  2. Altitude to the Hypotenuse: In a right triangle, the altitude drawn to the hypotenuse creates two smaller right triangles. All three triangles (the original and the two smaller ones) are similar. This configuration also yields the Geometric Mean Theorem: if the hypotenuse is divided into segments of length $p$ and $q$, then the altitude $h$ satisfies $h^2 = p \cdot q$.

7.5.1 — Congruent Triangles

Congruent triangles are identical in both shape and size. All corresponding sides and angles are equal.

Congruence Criteria:

  • SSS: All three corresponding sides are equal.
  • SAS: Two corresponding sides and the included angle are equal.
  • **ASA and the included side are equal.
  • AAS: Two corresponding angles and a non-included side are equal.
  • HL (Hypotenuse-Leg): In right triangles, if the hypotenuse and one leg are equal, the triangles are congruent.

Important: AAA establishes similarity, not congruence. SSA does not guarantee congruence (the "ambiguous case").

7.6 — Polygons

7.6.1 — Polygon Angle Sum Formula

The sum of the interior angles of an $n$-sided polygon:

$$\text{Sum of Interior Angles} = (n - 2) \times 180^\circ$$

Derivation: Any $n$-gon can be divided into $(n - 2)$ triangles by drawing diagonals from a single vertex. Since each triangle has an angle sum of $180^\circ$, the total is $(n - 2) \times 180^\circ$.

Polygon Sides ($n$) Sum of Interior Angles
Triangle 3 $180^\circ$
Quadrilateral 4 $360^\circ$
Pentagon 5 $540^\circ$
Hexagon 6 $720^\circ$
Octagon 8 $1080^\circ$
Decagon 10 $1440^\circ$

7.6.2 — Regular Polygons

A regular polygon has all sides congruent and all angles congruent.

$$\text{Each Interior Angle} = \frac{(n - 2) \times 180^\circ}{n}$$

$$\text{Each Exterior Angle} = \frac{360^\circ}{n}$$

Key Fact: The sum of the exterior angles of any polygon (regular or not), taking one exterior angle at each vertex, is always $360^\circ$.

7.6.3 — Quadrilaterals

Parallelogram

A quadrilateral with both pairs of opposite sides parallel.

Properties: - Opposite sides are parallel and congruent. - Opposite angles are congruent. - Consecutive angles are supplementary. - Diagonals bisect each other. - Area $= bh$ (base $\times$ height).

Rectangle

A parallelogram with four right angles.

Properties: - All properties of a parallelogram apply. - All angles are $90^\circ$. - Diagonals are congruent. - Area $= lw$. - Perimeter $= 2l + 2w$.

Rhombus

A parallelogram with four congruent sides.

Properties: - All properties of a parallelogram apply. - All sides are congruent. - Diagonals are perpendicular. - Diagonals bisect the interior angles. - Area $= \frac{1}{2}d_1d_2$ (half the product of the diagonals).

Square

A parallelogram that is both a rectangle and a rhombus. It has four right angles and four congruent sides.

Properties: - All properties of parallelograms, rectangles, and rhombuses apply. - Area $= s^2$. - Perimeter $= 4s$. - Diagonal $= s\sqrt{2}$.

Trapezoid

A quadrilateral with exactly one pair of parallel sides.

Properties: - The parallel sides are called bases. - The non-parallel sides are called legs. - The median (midsegment) connects the midpoints of the legs and is parallel to the bases. Its length equals the average of the bases: $m = \frac{b_1 + b_2}{2}$. - Area $= \frac{1}{2}(b_1 + b_2)h = mh$.

Isosceles Trapezoid: A trapezoid with congruent legs. The base angles are congruent, and the diagonals are congruent.

7.6.4 — Hierarchy of Quadrilaterals

All Quadrilaterals
├── Trapezoids (one pair of parallel sides)
└── Parallelograms (two pairs of parallel sides)
    ├── Rectangles (four right angles)
    │   └── Squares (four right angles + four congruent sides)
    └── Rhombuses (four congruent sides)
        └── Squares (four congruent sides + four right angles)

A square is both a rectangle and a rhombus. Therefore, every property of rectangles and rhombuses applies to squares.

7.7 — Circles

7.7.1 — Circle Terminology

  • Center: The point equidistant from all points on the circle.
  • Radius ($r$): The distance from the center to any point on the circle.
  • Diameter ($d$): A segment passing through the center with endpoints on the circle. $d = 2r$.
  • Chord: A segment with both endpoints on the circle. The diameter is the longest chord.
  • Secant Line: A line that intersects the circle at two points.
  • Tangent Line: A line that touches the circle at exactly one point (the point of tangency). The radius to the point of tangency is perpendicular to the tangent line.
  • Arc: A continuous part of the circle, measured in degrees.
  • Sector: The region bounded by two radii and the intercepted arc.
  • Segment: The region bounded by a chord and its intercepted arc.

7.7.2 — Essential Circle Formulas

Measurement Formula
Circumference $C = 2\pi r = \pi d$
Area $A = \pi r^2$
Arc Length $L = \frac{\theta}{360^\circ} \times 2\pi r$
Sector Area $A = \frac{\theta}{360^\circ} \times \pi r^2$

Critical Note: The arc length and sector area formulas are not provided on the SAT reference sheet. Both use the fraction $\frac{\theta}{360^\circ}$ — the fraction of the full circle represented by the central angle.

7.7.3 — Central Angles

A central angle has its vertex at the center of the circle. Its measure equals the measure of its intercepted arc.

7.7.4 — Inscribed Angles

An inscribed angle has its vertex on the circle and its sides are chords.

The Inscribed Angle Theorem: The measure of an inscribed angle is half the measure of its intercepted arc (and half the measure of the corresponding central angle).

$$\text{Inscribed Angle} = \frac{1}{2} \times \text{Intercepted Arc}$$

Corollaries: 1. Angles subtending the same arc are congruent. 2. An angle inscribed in a semicircle is a right angle ($90^\circ$). This is Thales' Theorem.

7.7.5 — Angles Formed by Chords, Secants, and Tangents

  • Two chords intersecting inside a circle: The angle formed equals half the sum of the intercepted arcs.
  • Two secants (or secant and tangent, or two tangents) intersecting outside a circle: The angle formed equals half the difference of the intercepted arcs.

7.7.6 — The Tangent-Radius Theorem

At the point of tangency, the radius of the circle is perpendicular to the tangent line. This creates a right angle, enabling the use of the Pythagorean Theorem.

7.7.7 — Tangent Segment Theorem

If two tangent segments are drawn from the same external point to a circle, they are congruent in length.

7.7.8 — The Intersecting Chords Theorem

If two chords intersect inside a circle, the products of the segments of each chord are equal.

If chords $AB$ and $CD$ intersect at point $P$:

$$AP \cdot PB = CP \cdot PD$$

7.7.9 — The Intersecting Secants Theorem

If two secants intersect outside the circle at point $P$, with the secants intersecting the circle at $A$, $B$ and $C$, $D$ respectively (where $A$ and $C$ are closer to $P$):

$$PA \cdot PB = PC \cdot PD$$

7.7.10 — The Secant-Tangent Theorem

If a tangent segment $PT$ and a secant $PAB$ intersect at external point $P$:

$$PT^2 = PA \cdot PB$$

7.7.11 — The Equation of a Circle

In the coordinate plane, a circle with center $(h, k)$ and radius $ equation:

$$(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2$$

Key Insight: The center is $(h, k)$. Note the minus signs. If the equation is $(x - 3)^2 + (y + 2)^2 = 25$, the center is $(3, -2)$ and the radius is $5$.

If the center is at the origin $(0, 0)$, the equation simplifies to $x^2 + y^2 = r^2$.

7.8 — Circle and Polygon Interactions

7.8.1 — Right Triangle Inscribed in a Circle

By Thales' Theorem, if a triangle is inscribed in a circle with one side as the diameter, the triangle is a right triangle and the diameter is the hypotenuse.

  • Hypotenuse $= 2r$
  • Radius $= \frac{1}{2} \times$ Hypotenuse

7.8.2 — Square Inscribed in a Circle

If a square is inscribed in a circle, the diagonal of the square equals the diameter of the circle.

  • Diagonal $= 2r$
  • Side length: $s\sqrt{2} = 2r$, so $s = r\sqrt{2}$
  • Area of inscribed square $= s^2 = 2r^2$

7.8.3 — Circle Inscribed in a Square

If a circle is inscribed in a square, the diameter of the circle equals the side length of the square.

  • Diameter $= s$
  • Radius $= \frac{s}{2}$
  • Area of circle $= \pi\left(\frac{s}{2}\right)^2 = \frac{\pi s^2}{4}$
  • Ratio of circle area to square area $= \frac{\pi}{4}$

7.8.4 — Equilateral Triangle Inscribed in a Circle

If an equilateral triangle is inscribed in a circle of radius $r$:

  • Side length: $s = r\sqrt{3}$
  • Height: $h = \frac{3r}{2}$
  • Area: $A = \frac{3r^2\sqrt{3}}{4}$

7.9 — Solid Geometry

Solid geometry on the SAT typically appears in only $1\text{--}2$ questions, but these questions are often straightforward if the formulas are known.

7.9.1 — Key 3D Shapes and Formulas

Rectangular Prism

  • Volume: $V = lwh$
  • Surface Area: $SA = 2lw + 2lh + 2wh$
  • Space Diagonal: $d = \sqrt{l^2 + w^2 + h^2}$

Cube (side length $s$)

  • Volume: $V = s^3$
  • Surface Area: $SA = 6s^2$
  • Space Diagonal: $d = s\sqrt{3}$
  • Face Diagonal: $d = s\sqrt{2}$

Cylinder

  • Volume: $V = \pi r^2 h$
  • Surface Area: $SA = 2\pi r^2 + 2\pi rh$ (two circular bases + lateral surface)
  • Lateral Surface Area: $2\pi rh$

Sphere

  • Volume: $V = \frac{4}{3}\pi r^3$
  • Surface Area: $SA = 4\pi r^2$

Cone

  • Volume: $V = \frac{1}{3}\pi r^2 h$
  • Note: The volume of a cone is one-third the volume of a cylinder with the same base and height.

Pyramid

  • Volume: $V = \frac{1}{3}Bh$ where $B$ is the area of the base
  • Note: The volume of a pyramid is one-third the volume of a prism with the same base and height.

7.9.2 — Inscribed Solids

  • Sphere inscribed in a cube: The diameter of the sphere equals the side length of the cube. The sphere touches the center of each face.
  • Cube inscribed in a sphere: The space diagonal of the cube equals the diameter of the sphere. If the cube has side $s$, the sphere's diameter is $s\sqrt{3}$.

7.9.3 — Scaling in Three Dimensions

When a $3$D figure is scaled by a factor $k$:

  • All linear dimensions scale by $k$.
  • All surface areas scale by $k^2$.
  • All volumes scale by $k^3$.

Example: If the radius of a sphere is doubled, its volume increases by a factor of $2^3 = 8$.

7.10 — Coordinate Geometry

7.10.1 — The Distance Formula

The distance between two1, y_1)$ and $(x_2, y_2)$:

$$d = \sqrt{(x_2 - x_1)^2 + (y_2 - y_1)^2}$$

This is the Pythagorean Theorem applied to the horizontal and vertical differences between the points.

7.10.2 — The Midpoint Formula

The midpoint of the segment connecting $(x_1, y_1)$ and $(x_2, y_2)$:

$$M = \left(\frac{x_1 + x_2}{2}, \frac{y_1 + y_2}{2}\right)$$

This is the average of the $x$-coordinates and the average of the $y$-coordinates.

7.10.3 — Slope and Its Geometric Meaning

The slope of a line:

$$m = \frac{y_2 - y_1}{x_2 - x_1} = \frac{\text{rise}}{\text{run}}$$

  • Positive slope: Line rises from left to right.
  • Negative slope: Line falls from left to right.
  • Zero slope: Horizontal line.
  • Undefined slope: Vertical line.

7.10.4 — Parallel and Perpendicular Lines

  • Parallel lines have the same slope.
  • Perpendicular lines have slopes that are negative reciprocals (their product is $-1$).

7.10.5 — Circle Equation in the Coordinate Plane

$$(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2$$

To find where a line intersects a circle, substitute the linear equation into the circle equation. The resulting quadratic's discriminant tells us the number of intersection points:

  • Positive discriminant: $2$ intersection points (secant line).
  • Zero discriminant: $1$ intersection point (tangent line).
  • Negative discriminant: $0$ intersection points (line misses the circle).

7.11 — Trigonometry: Right Triangle Ratios

Trigonometry on the SAT is limited to right triangle trigonometry using SOH-CAH-TOA.

7.11.1 — The Three Primary Trigonometric Ratios

In a right triangle, for an acute angle $\theta$:

Function Abbreviation Ratio
Sine $\sin(\theta)$ $\frac{\text{Opposite}}{\text{Hypotenuse}}$
Cosine $\cos(\theta)$ $\frac{\text{Adjacent}}{\text{Hypotenuse}}$
Tangent $\tan(\theta)$ $\frac{\text{Opposite}}{\text{Adjacent}}$

Mnemonic: SOH-CAH-TOA

Critical Nuance: The labels "Opposite" and "Adjacent" depend on which acute angle is the reference angle. The hypotenuse is always opposite the $90^\circ$ angle and never changes.

7.11.2 — The Reciprocal Functions

Function Abbreviation Ratio
Cosecant $\csc(\theta)$ $\frac{\text{Hypotenuse}}{\text{Opposite}} = \frac{1}{\sin(\theta)}$
Secant $\sec(\theta)$ $\frac{\text{Hypotenuse}}{\text{Adjacent}} = \frac{1}{\cos(\theta)}$
Cotangent $\cot(\theta)$ $\frac{\text{Adjacent}}{\text{Opposite}} = \frac{1}{\tan(\theta)}$

7.11.3 — The Pythagorean Identity

$$\sin^2\theta + \cos^2\theta = 1$$

This identity holds for any angle $\theta$, derived by applying the Pythagorean Theorem to a right triangle and dividing by the hypotenuse squared.

Application: If $\sin(\theta) = \frac{3}{5}$, then:

$$\left(\frac{3}{5}\right)^2 + \cos^2\theta = 1$$ $$\frac{9}{25} + \cos^2\theta = 1$$ $$\cos^2\theta = \frac{16}{25}$$ $$\cos(\theta) = \frac{4}{5} \quad (\text{for acute } \theta)$$

7.11.4 — The Cofunction Identity

$$\sin(\theta) = \cos(90^\circ - \theta)$$ $$\cos(\theta) = \sin(90^\circ - \theta)$$

This reflects the fact that in a right triangle, the two acute angles are complementary. The sine of one acute angle equals the cosine of the other.

7.11.5 — Standard Trig Values to Memorize

These values derive from the $30^\circ\text{-}60^\circ\text{-}90^\circ$ and $45^\circ\text{-}45^\circ\text{-}90^\circ$ triangles.

Angle $\sin$ $\cos$ $\tan$
$30^\circ$ $\frac{1}{2}$ $\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}$ $\frac{\sqrt{3}}{3}$
$45^\circ$ $\frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}$ $\frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}$ $1$
$60^\circ$ $\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}$ $\frac{1}{2}$ $\sqrt{3}$

Memory Hook: For sine at $30^\circ$, $45^\circ$, and $60^\circ$, the sequence is $\frac{\sqrt{1}}{2}$, $\frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}$, $\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}$. Cosine follows this sequence in reverse.

7.11.6 — Using Trigonometry to Solve Problems

Finding a Missing Side:

  1. Identify the reference angle.
  2. Label the sides (opposite, adjacent, hypotenuse) relative to that angle.
  3. Choose the trig function that incorporates the known side and the unknown side.
  4. Set up the equation and solve.

Finding a Missing Angle (Inverse Trigonometry):

  1. Identify the two known sides.
  2. Choose the trig function using those two sides.
  3. Apply the inverse function:

$$\theta = \sin^{-1}!\left(\frac{\text{opposite}}{\text{hypotenuse}}\right), \quad \theta = \cos^{-1}!\left(\frac{\text{adjacent}}{\text{hypotenuse}}\right), \quad \theta = \tan^{-1}!\left(\frac{\text{opposite}}{\text{adjacent}}\right)$$

7.12 — Scaling Laws for Similar Figures

When two figures are similar with a scale factor of $k$ (the ratio of corresponding lengths):

Property Scale Factor
Corresponding lengths (sides, perimeters, radii) $k$
Areas (surface areas) $k^2$
Volumes $k^3$

Working Backward: If the ratio of areas is $k^2$, the ratio of corresponding lengths is $\sqrt{k^2} = k$. If the ratio of volumes is $k^3$, the ratio of corresponding lengths is $\sqrt[3]{k^3} = k$.

Example: If the area of figure $A$ is $4$ times the area of figure $B$, then the side length of $A$ is $\sqrt{4} = 2$ times the side length of $B$.

7.13 — Geometric Proportionality Theorems

7.13.1 — The Angle Bisector Theorem

In a triangle, the angle bisector of an angle divides the opposite side into segments proportional to the adjacent sides.

If in $\triangle ABC$, the angle bisector of $\angle A$ meets side $BC$ at point $D$:

$$\frac{BD}{DC} = \frac{AB}{AC}$$

7.13.2 — The Triangle Proportionality Theorem

If a line parallel to one side of a triangle intersects the other two sides, it divides those sides proportionally.

If $DE \parallel BC$ in $\triangle ABC$:

$$\frac{AD}{DB} = \frac{AE}{EC}$$

7.14 — Comprehensive Formula Reference

Lines and Angles

Concept Relationship

Sum of interior angles of $n$-gon $(n - 2) \times 180^\circ$
Each interior angle of regular $n$-gon $\frac{(n-2) \times 180^\circ}{n}$
Each exterior angle of regular $n$-gon $\frac{360^\circ}{n}$
Sum of exterior angles $360^\circ$

Triangles

Triangle inequality $b - a < c < a + b$
Pythagorean theorem $a^2 + b^2 = c^2$

$30^\circ\text{-}60^\circ\text{-}90^\circ$ ratio $s : s\sqrt{3} : 2s$
Area (general) $\frac{1}{2}bh$
Area (equilateral) $\frac{s^2\sqrt{3}}{4}$

Cylinder $\pi r^2 h$ $2\pi r^2 + 2\pi rh$
Sphere $\frac{4}{3}\pi r^3$ $4\pi r^2$
Cone $\frac{1}{3}\pi r^2 h$ —
Pyramid $\frac{1}{3}Bh$ —

Coordinate Geometry

Concept Formula
Distance $\sqrt{(x_2-x_1)^2 + (y_2-y_1)^2}$
Midpoint $\left(\frac{x_1+x_2}{2}, \frac{y_1+y_2}{2}\right)$
Slope $\frac{y_2-y_1}{x_2-x_1}$
Circle equation $(x-h)^2 + (y-k)^2 = r^2$

7.15 — Common SAT Geometry Traps and How to Avoid Them

Trap 1: Assuming Information Not Given

Never assume a figure is regular, a line is parallel, or an angle is right unless explicitly stated or logically deducible.

Trap 2: Confusing Area and Perimeter

Always double-check what the question is asking for. The SAT frequently provides answer choices that represent the perimeter when the question asks for area (and vice versa).

Trap 3: Confusing Radius and Diameter

Always write down explicitly whether $r$ or $d$ is given before plugging into formulas. The most common error in circle problems is using $r$ when the problem gives $d$.

Trap 4: Misapplying the Pythagorean Theorem

The Pythagorean Theorem applies only to right triangles. Never apply it to acute or obtuse triangles.

Trap 5: Confusing Similarity and Congruence

Similar figures have proportional sides; congruent figures have equal sides. The SAT frequently tests the distinction.

Trap 6: Ignoring Units

Always ensure that quantities being compared or combined are in the same units before performing calculations.

Trap 7: Squaring Pi

$\pi r^2$ means $\pi \cdot r \cdot r$. It does not mean $(\pi r)^2$.

Trap 8: Assuming Figures Are Drawn to Scale

While most figures are drawn to scale, some are explicitly labeled "not drawn to scale." In these cases, rely only on given measurements and geometric properties.

7.16 — SAT-Specific Geometry Strategies

Exploiting Scale Diagrams

Most SAT geometry figures are drawn to scale. This means you can: - Estimate angle measures by visual comparison to known angles ($90^\circ$, $180^\circ$). - Estimate side lengths by comparing to known sides. - Eliminate impossible answer choices based on visual estimation.

Drawing Auxiliary Lines

Many geometry problems become solvable only after drawing an additional line — a diagonal, an altitude, a parallel line, or a radius. Common auxiliary lines include: - Drawing a diagonal in a quadrilateral to create triangles. - Drawing an altitude in a triangle to create right triangles. - Drawing a radius to a point of tangency (creating a right angle). - Drawing a line parallel to a given line through a specific point.

Breaking Complex Figures into Simpler Ones

Complex geometric figures can almost always be decomposed into triangles, rectangles, circles, and other basic shapes. The strategy is to identify the component shapes, find their individual measurements, and combine them appropriately.

The "Hidden Right Triangle" Strategy

Many circle problems do not explicitly draw a right angle, but one is hidden: - Tangent-Radius: A radius to a point of tangency creates a right angle. - Inscribed Angle on a Diameter: Thales' Theorem guarantees a right angle. - **Chord radius bisecting a chord is perpendicular to it.

Plugging in Numbers

When a geometry problem involves variables, assign a convenient value to the variable and work through the problem numerically. This is particularly useful for ratio and proportion problems.

7.17 — The Interconnected Nature of Geometry

Virtually every geometric concept connects to every other. A single problem might require recognizing a right triangle (Pythagorean Theorem), identifying it as a $30^\circ\text{-}60^\circ\text{-}90^\circ$ triangle (special right triangle ratios), using trigonometry to find an angle (SOH-CAH-TOA), applying the angle sum theorem to find another angle, using the inscribed angle theorem to relate it to a circle, and calculating the area of a sector.

This interconnectedness is what makes geometry both challenging and elegant. The key is to build a robust mental framework where every theorem, formula, and relationship is accessible and applicable.

End of Chapter 7

Chapter 8: Trigonometry and Circles — SOH-CAH-TOA, Identities, Arcs, Sectors, and Inscribed Figures

Introduction to the Chapter

Trigonometry and Circles represent two of the most visually intuitive yet algebraically rigorous domains on the SAT Math section. While they account for roughly 15% of the total math questions (approximately 5 to 7 questions), these questions are frequently the gatekeepers for top-tier scores. The Digital SAT's adaptive nature means that mastering these concepts in Module 1 is essential to unlocking the harder, higher-ceiling Module 2.

This chapter provides an exhaustive, textbook-level deep dive into the mechanics of right-triangle trigonometry, the geometric properties of circles, and the complex relationships between inscribed figures and their circumscribing shapes. We will move beyond mere memorization, exploring the why behind every formula, the how of every identity, and the strategy for every problem archetype.

Part I: The Architecture of Right-Triangle Trigonometry

Trigonometry, at its core on the SAT, is the study of ratios within right triangles. Unlike advanced trigonometry courses that explore the unit circle, periodic functions, and radian measures, the SAT restricts its trigonometric testing to acute right triangles. This constraint makes the subject highly procedural, provided you have an unshakable grasp of the foundational ratios.

1. The Primary Ratios: SOH-CAH-TOA

The sine, cosine, and tangent functions are nothing more than specific ratios of the sides of a right triangle relative to a designated acute angle. Let us define these with absolute precision.

Consider a right triangle $ABC$, where angle $C$ is the right angle ($90^\circ$). Let $\theta$ (theta) represent one of the acute angles, say angle $A$. The sides of the triangle are defined relative to $\theta$: * Hypotenuse: The side opposite the right angle. It is always the longest side of the triangle. * Opposite: The side directly across from the angle $\theta$. * Adjacent: The side that forms the angle $\theta$ along with the hypotenuse. It is "next to" the angle but is not the hypotenuse.

The three primary trigonometric functions are defined as follows:

  • Sine ($\sin$): The ratio of the length of the Opposite side to the length of the Hypotenuse. $$\sin(\theta) = \frac{\text{Opposite}}{\text{Hypotenuse}}$$
  • Cosine ($\cos$): The ratio of the length of the Adjacent side to the length of the Hypotenuse. $$\cos(\theta) = \frac{\text{Adjacent}}{\text{Hypotenuse}}$$
  • Tangent ($\tan$): The ratio of the length of the Opposite side to the length of the Adjacent side. $$\tan(\theta) = \frac{\text{Opposite}}{\text{Adjacent}}$$

The Mnemonic: SOH-CAH-TOA. * Sine = Opposite / Hypotenuse * Cosine = Adjacent / Hypotenuse * Tangent = Opposite / Adjacent

Critical Nuance 1: The Reference Angle Matters. The labels "Opposite" and "Adjacent" are entirely dependent on which acute angle you are analyzing. If you switch your reference angle from $A$ to $B$, the side that was previously "Opposite" becomes "Adjacent," and vice versa. The Hypotenuse, however, never changes; it is always opposite the $90^\circ$ angle.

Critical Nuance 2: The Hypotenuse is Never the Opposite or Adjacent. A common error is to mislabel the hypotenuse as the adjacent side. Remember, the adjacent side must form one of the rays of the angle $\theta$. The hypotenuse and the adjacent side form the angle, but the hypotenuse is distinct from the adjacent side.

2. The Reciprocal Functions: CSC, SEC, COT

While the SAT primarily tests sine, cosine, and tangent, it occasionally tests the reciprocal functions. These are algebraically derived by flipping the fractions of the primary ratios.

  • Cosecant ($\csc$): The reciprocal of sine. $$\csc(\theta) = \frac{1}{\sin(\theta)} = \frac{\text{Hypotenuse}}{\text{Opposite}}$$
  • Secant ($\sec$): The reciprocal of cosine. $$\sec(\theta) = \frac{1}{\cos(\theta)} = \frac{\text{Hypotenuse}}{\text{Adjacent}}$$
  • Cotangent ($\cot$): The reciprocal of tangent. $$\cot(\theta) = \frac{1}{\tan(\theta)} = \frac{\text{Adjacent}}{\text{Opposite}}$$

Mnemonic: The reciprocal functions are "co-" functions of the primary reciprocals. Notice that Cosecant is the reciprocal of Sine (not Cosine), Secant is the reciprocal of Cosine (not Sine), and Cotangent is the reciprocal of Tangent.

3. Solving for Missing Sides and Angles

Trigonometric functions are equations. If you know the angle and one side, you can solve for an unknown side. If you know two sides, you can solve for an unknown angle using inverse functions.

Strategy for Finding a Missing Side

  1. Identify the Reference Angle: Determine which acute angle the problem is focusing on.
  2. Label the Sides: Mark the sides as Opposite, Adjacent, and Hypotenuse relative to that angle.
  3. Choose the Function: Select the trig function that incorporates the known side and the unknown side.
    • If you know the Hypotenuse and need the Opposite, use Sine.
    • If you know the Adjacent and need the Hypotenuse, use Cosine.
    • If you know the Opposite and need the Adjacent, use Tangent.
  4. Set Up the Equation: Substitute the known values and the variable for the unknown side into the ratio.
  5. Solve Algebraically: Treat the trig function as a coefficient. For example, if $\sin(30^\circ) = \frac{x}{10}$, then $x = 10 \cdot \sin(30^\circ)$.

Strategy for Finding a Missing Angle (Inverse Trigonometry)

When you know the ratio of two sides but not the angle itself, you must use the inverse trigonometric functions, denoted as $\sin^{-1}$, $\cos^{-1}$, and $\tan^{-1}$ (also called arcsin, arccos, arctan).

  1. Identify the Known Sides: Determine which two sides are given in the problem.
  2. Choose the Function: Select the trig function that uses those two sides.
  3. Set Up the Ratio: Write the ratio of the sides.
  4. Apply the Inverse Function: To find the angle $\theta$, apply the inverse function to the ratio. $$\theta = \sin^{-1}\left(\frac{\text{Opposite}}{\text{Hypotenuse}}\right)$$ $$\theta = \cos^{-1}\left(\frac{\text{Adjacent}}{\text{Hypotenuse}}\right)$$ $$\theta = \tan^{-1}\left(\frac{\text{Opposite}}{\text{Adjacent}}\right)$$

Calculator Note: On the Digital SAT, you will use the Desmos calculator or your handheld calculator to compute inverse trig functions. Ensure your calculator is in Degree Mode, not Radian Mode, unless the problem explicitly specifies radians.

4. The Pythagorean Identity

The Pythagorean Theorem ($a^2 + b^2 = c^2$) and the trigonometric functions are inextricably linked. By dividing the Pythagorean Theorem by $c^2$ (the hypotenuse squared), we derive the fundamental Pythagorean Identity:

$$\sin^2(\theta) + \cos^2(\theta) = 1$$

Derivation: 1. Start with $a^2 + b^2 = c^2$. 2. Divide every term by $c^2$: $\frac{a^2}{c^2} + \frac{b^2}{c^2} = \frac{c^2}{c^2}$. 3. Simplify: $\left(\frac{a}{c}\right)^2 + \left(\frac{b}{c}\right)^2 = 1$. 4. Substitute the trig definitions: $\frac{a}{c} = \sin(\theta)$ and $\frac{b}{c} = \cos(\theta)$. 5. Result: $\sin^2(\theta) + \cos^2(\theta) = 1$.

Application: If you are given $\sin(\theta) = \frac{3}{5}$ and asked to find $\cos(\theta)$ (assuming $\theta$ is acute), you do not need to construct a triangle. You simply substitute: $$\left(\frac{3}{5}\right)^2 + \cos^2(\theta) = 1$$ $$\frac{9}{25} + \cos^2(\theta) = 1$$ $$\cos^2(\theta) = \frac{16}{25}$$ $$\cos(\theta) = \frac{4}{5}$$

There are two other Pythagorean identities derived by dividing the original theorem by $a^2$ or $b^2$, though they are rarely tested on the SAT: * $1 + \tan^2(\theta) = \sec^2(\theta)$ * $1 + \cot^2(\theta) = \csc^2(\theta)$

5. The Cofunction Relationship

In a right triangle, the two acute angles are complementary (they sum to $90^\circ$). This geometric reality creates a profound algebraic relationship between sine and cosine known as the Cofunction Identity.

$$\sin(\theta) = \cos(90^\circ - \theta)$$ $$\cos(\theta) = \sin(90^\circ - \theta)$$

Explanation: Consider a right triangle with angles $A$, $B$, and $C$ (where $C = 90^\circ$). The side opposite angle $A$ is the same side adjacent to angle $B$. Therefore, $\sin(A) = \frac{\text{Opposite to A}}{\text{Hypotenuse}}$ and $\cos(B) = \frac{\text{Adjacent to B}}{\text{Hypotenuse}}$. Since the side opposite $A$ is adjacent to $B$, these ratios are identical. Because $A + B = 90^\circ$, $B = 90^\circ - A$. Thus, $\sin(A) = \cos(90^\circ - A)$.

Strategic Application: If a problem asks you to solve an equation like $\sin(2x) = \cos(3x - 10)$, you can use the cofunction identity to rewrite it as $\sin(2x) = \sin(90^\circ - (3x - 10))$, which simplifies to $2x = 100 - 3x$, allowing you to solve for $x$ algebraically.

6. Standard Trig Values to Memorize

While the SAT provides a reference sheet, relying on it for basic trig values wastes precious seconds. You must have the sine, cosine, and tangent values for $30^\circ$, $45^\circ$, and $60^\circ$ memorized instantly. These values derive from the Special Right Triangles (which we will revisit in Part II).

Angle ($\theta$) $\sin(\theta)$ $\cos(\theta)$ $\tan(\theta)$
$30^\circ$ $\frac{1}{2}$ $\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}$ $\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}} = \frac{\sqrt{3}}{3}$
$45^\circ$ $\frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}$ $\frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}$ $1$
$60^\circ$ $\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}$ $\frac{1}{2}$ $\sqrt{3}$

Memory Hook for Sine and Cosine: Notice the pattern for sine: $\frac{\sqrt{1}}{2}, \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}, \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}$ (for $30^\circ, 45^\circ, 60^\circ$). Cosine is this exact sequence in reverse order.

Part II: The Geometry of Circles

A circle is defined as the set of all points in a plane that are equidistant from a central point. That fixed distance is the radius ($r$). The distance across the circle through the center is the diameter ($d = 2r$).

1. Foundational Circle Formulas

These formulas are provided on the SAT reference sheet, but you must be able to manipulate them fluently.

  • Circumference ($C$): The perimeter of the circle. $$C = 2\pi r = \pi d$$
  • Area ($A$): The space enclosed within the circle. $$A = \pi r^2$$

Critical Distinction: Circumference is a linear measurement (units), while area is a square measurement (units$^2$). A common trap is squaring the radius when calculating circumference, or failing to square it when calculating area.

2. Arcs and Sectors: Fractions of a Circle

An arc is a portion of the circumference. A sector is a portion of the area, bounded by two radii and an arc. Both are defined by a central angle ($\theta$) measured in degrees.

The fundamental principle governing arcs and sectors is that the ratio of the arc length (or sector area) to the total circumference (or total area) is equal to the ratio of the central angle to the total degrees in a circle ($360^\circ$).

$$\frac{\text{Arc Length}}{\text{Circumference}} = \frac{\theta}{360^\circ}$$ $$\frac{\text{Sector Area}}{\text{Circle Area}} = \frac{\theta}{360^\circ}$$

From these proportions, we derive the explicit formulas:

  • Arc Length ($L$): $$L = \frac{\theta}{360^\circ} \cdot 2\pi r$$
  • Sector Area ($A_s$): $$A_s = \frac{\theta}{360^\circ} \cdot \pi r^2$$

Deep Dive into Radians: While the SAT primarily uses degrees, you may occasionally encounter radians. A radian is an angle measurement defined by the radius. One radian is the angle created when the arc length equals the radius. Since the full circumference is $2\pi r$, there are $2\pi$ radians in a full circle ($360^\circ$). * Conversion: $360^\circ = 2\pi \text{ radians} \implies 180^\circ = \pi \text{ radians}$. * Radian Arc Length Formula: If $\theta$ is in radians, the arc length formula simplifies beautifully to $L = r\theta$. (This is actually the definition of a radian). * Radian Sector Area Formula: If $\theta$ is in radians, the sector area formula simplifies to $A_s = \frac{1}{2}r^2\theta$.

3. Inscribed Angles and the Inscribed Angle Theorem

An inscribed angle is an angle whose vertex lies on the circle and whose sides contain chords of the circle. The Inscribed Angle Theorem is one of the most frequently tested circle theorems on the SAT.

The Theorem: The measure of an inscribed angle is exactly half the measure of its intercepted arc (and consequently, half the measure of the central angle that subtends the same arc).

$$\text{Inscribed Angle} = \frac{1}{2} \cdot \text{Intercepted Arc}$$

Proof/Visualization: Consider an inscribed angle $\angle$ intercepting arc $BC$. Draw a diameter from $A$ through the center $O$, hitting the circle at $D$. This creates two isosceles triangles, $\triangle OAB$ and $\triangle OAC$. Using the exterior angle theorem and the fact that the central angle $\angle BOC$ equals the measure of arc $BC$, we can algebraically prove that $\angle BAC = \frac{1}{2} \angle BOC$.

Corollaries of the Inscribed Angle Theorem: 1. Angles subtending the same arc are equal. If two inscribed angles intercept the same arc, they must be congruent. 2. An angle inscribed in a semicircle is a right angle. If an inscribed angle intercepts an arc of $180^\circ$ (a semicircle), the angle measures $\frac{1}{2}(180^\circ) = 90^\circ$. This is the foundation for Thales' Theorem.

4. Circle Equation in Coordinate Geometry

In the coordinate plane, a circle is defined by its center $(h, k)$ and its radius $r$. The standard form equation of a circle is derived directly from the distance formula.

$$(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2$$

  • $(h, k)$ represents the coordinates of the center.
  • $r$ is the radius.
  • Note the subtraction signs: if the equation is $(x + 3)^2 + (y - 2)^2 = 25$, the center is $(-3, 2)$ and the radius is $\sqrt{25} = 5$.

General Form: Sometimes the equation is expanded into general form: $x^2 + y^2 + Dx + Ey + F = 0$. To find the center and radius from general form, you must complete the square for both the $x$ and $y$ terms to convert it back to standard form.

Intersection of a Line and a Circle: To find where a line intersects a circle, substitute the linear equation into the circle equation. This will result in a quadratic equation. The discriminant of this quadratic tells you the number of intersection points: * Positive discriminant: 2 intersection points (secant line). * Zero discriminant: 1 intersection point (tangent line). * Negative discriminant: 0 intersection points (line misses the circle).

Part III: Inscribed Figures and Composite Shapes

The SAT loves to test the relationships between circles and polygons (triangles, squares) that are inscribed within them or circumscribed around them. These problems require you to link the dimensions of the polygon to the radius of the circle.

1. Right Triangle Inscribed in a Circle (Thales' Theorem)

If a right triangle is inscribed in a circle, the hypotenuse of the triangle is always a diameter of the circle.

  • Hypotenuse = Diameter = $2r$
  • Radius = $\frac{1}{2}$ Hypotenuse

Conversely: If you know the hypotenuse of a right triangle, you can find the area of the circumscribing circle. If the hypotenuse is $c$, then $2r = c \implies r = \frac{c}{2}$. The area of the circle is $\pi \left(\frac{c}{2}\right)^2 = \frac{\pi c^2}{4}$.

2. Square Inscribed in a Circle

When a square is inscribed in a circle, the vertices of the square touch the circle. The diagonal of the square is equal to the diameter of the circle.

  • Diagonal of square = Diameter of circle = $2r$
  • Relationship to side length ($s$): The diagonal of a square is $s\sqrt{2}$.
  • Therefore: $s\sqrt{2} = 2r \implies s = \frac{2r}{\sqrt{2}} = r\sqrt{2}$.
  • Area of the inscribed square: $s^2 = (r\sqrt{2})^2 = 2r^2$.

Strategic Application: If a problem gives you the area of the circle ($\pi r^2$) and asks for the area of the inscribed square ($2r^2$), you can find the ratio. The ratio of the square's area to the circle's area is $\frac{2r^2}{\pi r^2} = \frac{2}{\pi}$.

3. Circle Inscribed in a Square

When a circle is inscribed in a square, the circle touches the square at the midpoint of each side. The diameter of the circle is equal to the side length of the square.

  • Diameter of circle = Side of square = $s$
  • Radius of circle = $\frac{s}{2}$
  • Area of the circle: $\pi \left(\frac{s}{2}\right)^2 = \frac{\pi s^2}{4}$.
  • Area of the square: $s^2$.
  • Ratio of Circle Area to Square Area: $\frac{\pi}{4}$.

4. Equilateral Triangle Inscribed in a Circle

This is a more complex relationship that often appears in harder problems. When an equilateral triangle is inscribed in a circle, the center of the circle is the centroid of the triangle.

  • Radius ($r$) and Side Length ($s$): The relationship is $s = r\sqrt{3}$.
  • Derivation: The central angle subtended by one side of the equilateral triangle is $120^\circ$ ($360^\circ / 3$). If you drop a perpendicular from the center to the midpoint of a side, you bisect the central angle and the side. This creates a $30^\circ-60^\circ-90^\circ$ triangle where the hypotenuse is $r$, the short leg is $r/2$, and the long leg is $\frac{r\sqrt{3}}{2}$. The long leg is half the side of the equilateral triangle. So, $\frac{s}{2} = \frac{r\sqrt{3}}{2} \implies s = r\sqrt{3}$.
  • Height ($h$) of the triangle: The height of the equilateral triangle is the sum of the long leg of the $30^\circ-60^\circ-90^\circ$ triangle and the radius. Actually, the centroid divides the height in a $2:1$ ratio. The distance from the center to the vertex is $r$ (which is $\frac{2}{3}h$), and the distance from the center to the side is $\frac{1}{3}h$. Thus, $r = \frac{2}{3}h \implies h = \frac{3r}{2}$.
  • Area of the equilateral triangle: Using the standard formula $\frac{s^2\sqrt{3}}{4}$ and substituting $s = r\sqrt{3}$, we get $\frac{(r\sqrt{3})^2\sqrt{3}}{4} = \frac{3r^2\sqrt{3}}{4}$.

5. Overlapping Shapes and Shaded Regions

A classic SAT archetype involves finding the area of a shaded region created by overlapping geometric figures. The universal strategy is the Additive/Subtractive Method.

  1. Identify the Outer Boundary: What is the total shape encompassing the shaded region?
  2. Identify the Inner Holes: What shapes are cut out or overlapping?
  3. Set up the Equation: $\text{Shaded Area} = \text{Area of Outer Shape} - \text{Area of Inner Shape(s)}$.

Example Archetype: A square is inscribed in a circle. Find the area of the region inside the circle but outside the square. * Outer Shape: Circle ($\pi r^2$) * Inner Shape: Square ($2r^2$) * Shaded Area: $\pi r^2 - 2r^2 = r^2(\pi - 2)$.

Part IV: Advanced Circle Theorems (Beyond the Basics)

While the previous sections cover the core of the SAT curriculum, the hardest Module 2 questions often require knowledge of more advanced circle theorems.

1. Tangent Lines

A tangent line is a line that touches a circle at exactly one point. The point of contact is the point of tangency.

The Tangent-Radius Theorem: At the point of tangency, the radius of the circle is perpendicular to the tangent line. * This creates a right angle ($90^\circ$). * This is crucial because it allows you to use the Pythagorean Theorem or trigonometry if a triangle is formed by the radius, the tangent segment, and a secant line from an external point.

Tangent Segment Theorem: If two tangent segments are drawn from the same external point to a circle, they are congruent in length. * If point $P$ is outside the circle, and $PA$ and $PB$ are tangent to the circle at $A$ and $B$, then $PA = PB$.

2. Chords and the Intersecting Chords Theorem

A chord is a line segment whose circle. The diameter is the longest possible chord.

Intersecting Chords Theorem: If two chords intersect inside a circle, the products of the segments of each chord are equal. * If chords $AB$ and $CD$ intersect at point $P$, then $AP \cdot PB = CP \cdot PD$.

Application: This theorem is essentially a similarity relationship in disguise. The triangles formed by these intersecting chords ($\triangle APC$ and $\triangle DPB$) are similar (by AA similarity: vertical angles are equal, and inscribed angles subtending the same arc are equal). The proportion $\frac{AP}{PD} = \frac{CP}{PB}$ cross-multiplies to give the theorem.

3. Secants and External Intersection

A secant is a line that intersects a circle at two points (a chord extended infinitely).

Intersecting Secants Theorem: If two secants intersect outside the circle, the product of the lengths of one secant segment and its external segment equals the product of the lengths of the other secant segment and its external segment. * If secants $PAB$ and $PCD$ intersect at external point $P$ (where $A$ and $C$ are closer to $P$), then $PA \cdot PB = PC \cdot PD$.

Secant-Tangent Theorem: If a tangent and a secant intersect outside the circle, the square of the length of the tangent segment is equal to the product of the lengths of the secant segment and its external segment. * If $PT$ is a tangent and $PAB$ is a secant, then $PT^2 = PA \cdot PB$.

Part V: Strategic Synthesis and Test-Taking Mastery

Knowing the formulas is necessary but not sufficient. The SAT is a reasoning test that uses these concepts as vehicles for testing logical deduction and strategic problem-solving.

1. The Desmos Advantage for Circles and Trig

The built-in Desmos calculator on the Digital SAT is a powerful tool for these topics.

  • Solving Trig Equations: If you need to solve $\sin(x) = 0.5$ for $x$ in degrees, you can graph $y = \sin(x)$ and $y = 0.5$ and find the intersection. (Remember to set the Desmos window to degrees if necessary, though Desmos defaults to radians for trig functions. You may need to use the degree symbol or convert).
  • Circle Equations: If a problem asks for the intersection of a line and a circle, graph both equations. The intersection points are your solutions. This is vastly faster than algebraic substitution and elimination.
  • Verifying Identities: If you are unsure if an identity holds, graph both sides of the equation. If the graphs overlap perfectly, the identity is true.

2. Estimation and Diagrams

The SAT explicitly states that figures are drawn to scale unless noted otherwise. For circle and trig problems, this is a massive advantage.

  • Angle Estimation: If you need to find $\sin(\theta)$ and the diagram shows $\theta$ is clearly less than $30^\circ$, you can eliminate answer choices that are greater than $0.5$.
  • Side Length Estimation: If a problem asks for the length of a chord, and you can see it is clearly longer than the radius but shorter than the diameter, you can eliminate answers outside the range $(r, 2r)$.
  • The "Not Drawn to Scale" Trap: If a figure is explicitly labeled "Not Drawn to Scale," do NOT trust your eyes. You must rely purely on the mathematical properties and given values.

3. Dimensional Analysis for Arcs and Sectors

When calculating arc length or sector area, use dimensional analysis to ensure you haven't made a mistake.

  • Arc Length: The formula is $\frac{\theta}{360} \cdot 2\pi r$. The $2\pi r$ term is a length (units). The fraction $\frac{\theta}{360}$ is unitless. Therefore, the result is a length (units). If you accidentally used the area formula $\pi r^2$, your result would be in units$^2$, which is physically impossible for a length.
  • Sector Area: The formula is $\frac{\theta}{360} \cdot \pi r^2$. The $\pi r^2$ term is an area (units$^2$). The result is an area.

4. The "Hidden Right Triangle" Strategy

Many circle problems do not explicitly draw a right angle, but one is hiding in plain sight.

  • Tangent-Radius: If a tangent is drawn, draw the radius to the point of tangency. You have just created a right angle.
  • Inscribed Angle/Hypotenuse: If a triangle is inscribed in a circle and one side is the diameter, you have a right triangle.
  • Chord Bisector: If a radius bisects a chord, it is perpendicular to the chord, creating two right triangles.

Whenever you encounter a circle problem, actively scan for these hidden right triangles. They are the key to unlocking the radius, the chord length, or the distance from the center to the chord.

5. Common Traps and Misconceptions

  • Confusing Radius and Diameter: The most common error in circle problems is using $r$ when the problem gives $d$, or vice versa. Always write down "r = ..." and "d = ..." explicitly before plugging into formulas.
  • Squaring Pi: $\pi r^2$ means $\pi \cdot r \cdot r$. It does NOT mean $(\pi r)^2$. The area of a circle with radius 2 is $\pi(2)^2 = 4\pi$, not $(2\pi)^2 = 4\pi^2$.
  • Degree vs. Radian Mode: If you are using your calculator to evaluate a trig function, ensure you are in the correct mode. If the angle is in degrees, your calculator must be in Degree mode. If the angle is in radians (e.g., $\frac{\pi}{3}$), it must be in Radian mode.
  • Inverse vs. Reciprocal: $\sin^{-1}(x)$ is the inverse sine (the angle whose sine is $x$). $(\sin(x))^{-1}$ or $\csc(x)$ is the reciprocal. These are fundamentally different operations.

Part VI: The Intersection of Trigonometry and Circles

The ultimate synthesis of this chapter occurs when trigonometry is applied to problems involving circles, particularly those with inscribed figures or central angles.

1. Finding Chord Length Using Trigonometry

If you know the radius $r$ and the central angle $\theta$ subtended by a chord, you can find the length of the chord without the Pythagorean Theorem by using the Law of Sines or basic right triangle trigonometry.

  1. Draw the central angle $\theta$ and the chord.
  2. Bisect the central angle with a line from the center to the midpoint of the chord. This creates two congruent right triangles.
  3. The hypotenuse of each right triangle is $r$.
  4. The angle at the center is $\frac{\theta}{2}$.
  5. Half the chord length is opposite the angle $\frac{\theta}{2}$.
  6. $\sin\left(\frac{\theta}{2}\right) = \frac{\text{Half Chord}}{r}$
  7. $\text{Chord Length} = 2r \sin\left(\frac{\theta}{2}\right)$

2. Finding the Apothem of an Inscribed Regular Polygon

The apothem is the distance from the center of a regular polygon to the midpoint of one of its sides. It is also the radius of the inscribed circle.

For a regular $n$-sided polygon inscribed in a circle of radius $r$: 1. The central angle subtended by one side is $\frac{360^\circ}{n}$. 2. The apothem bisects this central angle, creating a right triangle with angle $\frac{180^\circ}{n}$. 3. The hypotenuse is $r$. 4. The apothem is adjacent to the angle $\frac{180^\circ}{n}$. 5. $\cos\left(\frac{180^\circ}{n}\right) = \frac{\text{Apothem}}{r}$ 6. $\text{Apothem} = r \cos\left(\frac{180^\circ}{n}\right)$

Example: Find the apothem of a regular hexagon inscribed in a circle of radius 10. * $n = 6$. * Angle = $\frac{180^\circ}{6} = 30^\circ$. * Apothem = $10 \cos(30^\circ) = 10 \cdot \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} = 5\sqrt{3}$.

3. Area of a Regular Inscribed Polygon Using Trigonometry

The area of a regular $n$-gon inscribed in a circle of radius $r$ can be found by dividing the polygon into $n$ congruent isosceles triangles.

  1. Area triangle = $\frac{1}{2} \cdot \text{base} \cdot \text{height}$.
  2. The height is the apothem: $r \cos\left(\frac{180^\circ}{n}\right)$.
  3. The base is the side length: $2r \sin\left(\frac{180^\circ}{n}\right)$.
  4. Area of one triangle = $\frac{1}{2} \cdot \left[2r \sin\left(\frac{180^\circ}{n}\right)\right] \cdot \left[r \cos\left(\frac{180^\circ}{n}\right)\right] = r^2 \sin\left(\frac{180^\circ}{n}\right) \cos\left(\frac{180^\circ}{n}\right)$.
  5. Using the double-angle identity $\sin(2x) = 2\sin(x)\cos(x)$, we can rewrite this as $\frac{1}{2}r^2 \sin\left(\frac{360^\circ}{n}\right)$.
  6. Total Area = $n \cdot \frac{1}{2}r^2 \sin\left(\frac{360^\circ}{n}\right) = \frac{n}{2}r^2 \sin\left(\frac{360^\circ}{n}\right)$.

While this formula is rarely required in its full form on the SAT, understanding its derivation reinforces the deep connection between the circle's radius, the polygon's geometry, and trigonometric functions.

Part VII: Comprehensive Formula Sheet for Trigonometry and Circles

This section serves as a quick-reference summary of every formula discussed in this chapter. While some are provided on the SAT reference sheet, memorizing them saves time and builds confidence.

Trigonometry Formulas

Function Ratio Reciprocal
Sine $\sin(\theta) = \frac{O}{H}$ $\csc(\theta) = \frac{H}{O}$
Cosine $\cos(\theta) = \frac{A}{H}$ $\sec(\theta) = \frac{H}{A}$
Tangent $\tan(\theta) = \frac{O}{A}$ $\cot(\theta) = \frac{A}{O}$

Identities: * $\sin^2(\theta) + \cos^2(\theta) = 1$ * $\sin(\theta) = \cos(90^\circ - \theta)$ * $\tan(\theta) = \frac{\sin(\theta)}{\cos(\theta)}$

Standard Values:

$\theta$ $\sin(\theta)$ $\cos(\theta)$ $\tan(\theta)$
$30^\circ$ $\frac{1}{2}$ $\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}$ $\frac{\sqrt{3}}{3}$
$45^\circ$ $\frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}$ $\frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}$ $1$
$60^\circ$ $\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}$ $\frac{1}{2}$ $\sqrt{3}$

Circle Formulas

Measurement Formula
Area $A = \pi r^2$
Circumference $C = 2\pi r = \pi d$
Arc Length $L = \frac{\theta}{360} \cdot 2\pi r$ (degrees) or $L = r\theta$ (radians)
Sector Area $A_s = \frac{\theta}{360} \cdot \pi r^2$ (degrees) or $A_s = \frac{1}{2}r^2\theta$ (radians)
Inscribed Angle $\angle = \frac{1}{2} \cdot \text{Intercepted Arc}$
Standard Equation $(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2$

Inscribed Figure Relationships

Figure Key Relationship
Right Triangle Hypotenuse = Diameter ($2r$)
Square Diagonal = $2r$; Side = $r\sqrt{2}$; Area = $2r^2$
Circle in Square Diameter = Side ($s$); Radius = $s/2$
Equilateral $\Delta$ Side = $r\sqrt{3}$; Height = $\frac{3r}{2}$

Part VIII: Final Strategic Review

As you prepare for the SAT, keep the following overarching principles in mind:

  1. Trigonometry is a Tool, Not a Barrier: The SAT does not test advanced trig. If you see a trig problem, it is almost certainly a right-triangle problem in disguise. Label the sides, pick the ratio, and solve.
  2. Circles are Defined by the Radius: Almost every circle problem eventually funnels down to finding or using the radius. If you are stuck, ask yourself: "How can I find the radius?"
  3. Right Triangles are Everywhere: Between the Tangent-Radius theorem, Thales' theorem, and chord bisectors, right triangles are the secret skeleton key for circle geometry.
  4. Fractions are Your Friend: Arc length and sector area are just fractions of the whole. Thinking in terms of "what fraction of the circle is this?" can often lead to a faster solution than plugging into a formula.
  5. Desmos is a Lifesaver: For coordinate geometry circles and complex trig equations, graphing is often faster and less error-prone than algebra. Master the Desmos interface.

By internalizing the rigorous derivations, formulas, and strategies outlined in this chapter, you will be equipped to handle any trigonometry or circle problem the SAT throws at you, from the most basic Module 1 question to the most complex Module 2 challenge.

Chapter 9: Data Analysis, Statistics, and Probability — Measures of Center, Spread, Sampling, and Interpretation

9.1 — The SAT Landscape for Data Analysis and Statistics

The SAT Math section dedicates approximately 15% of its content to "Problem Solving and Data Analysis," which encompasses statistics, data interpretation, probability, and sampling methods. On a 44-question test, this translates to roughly 6–7 questions, many of which are deceptively simple in appearance but contain subtle traps that separate proficient readers from truly precise ones. The questions in this domain are unique on the SAT because they reward interpretation and reasoning more than raw computation. You will rarely be asked to perform a laborious calculation; instead, you will be asked to read a table, understand a distribution, interpret a measure, or evaluate a conclusion drawn from data.

This chapter will take you from the most fundamental definitions—mean, median, mode—through the most nuanced concepts—sampling bias, margin of error, experimental versus observational design—so that no question the College Board throws at you will feel unfamiliar.

9.2 — Measures of Center: Mean, Median, and Mode

9.2.1 — The Arithmetic Mean (Average)

The arithmetic mean is the most commonly used measure of center and the one most students feel comfortable with. However, the SAT specifically designs problems to exploit that very comfort, luring students into calculation traps when a conceptual understanding would save enormous time.

Definition: The mean of a data set is the sum of all values divided by the number of values.

$$\bar{x} = \frac{x_1 + x_2 + x_3 + \cdots + x_n}{n} = \frac{\sum_{i=1}^{n} x_i}{n}$$

where $\bar{x}$ denotes the sample mean, each $x_i$ is an individual data point, and $n$ is the total number of data points.

Critical Properties the SAT Tests:

  1. Sensitivity to outliers. The mean is pulled toward extreme values. If a data set is ${2, 3, 4, 5, 100}$, the mean is $22.8$, which does not represent any actual data point well. The median, by contrast, is $4$. The SAT loves asking questions like: "A company reports an average salary of $85,000. Most employees earn less than this. Why?" The answer is that a few very high salaries pull the mean upward.

  2. The mean as a balance point. Conceptually, the mean is the point on a number line where the data would balance if each data point had equal weight. This means the sum of the deviations (distances) below the mean equals the sum of the deviations above the mean:

$$\sum_{i=1}^{n}(x_i - \bar{x}) = 0$$

This property is occasionally tested in disguise. For example, if you are told the mean of five numbers is 20, and four of the numbers are 15, 18, 22, and 25, the fifth number must be $5 \times 20 - (15 + 18 + 22 + 25) = 100 - 80 = 20$.

  1. Weighted mean. When different data points carry different importance, you compute a weighted average:

$$\bar{x}_{\text{weighted}} = \frac{w_1 x_1 + w_2 x_2 + \cdots + w_n x_n}{w_1 + w_2 + \cdots + w_n}$$

This appears on the SAT in contexts like grade calculations (e.g., homework is 20%, midterm is 30%, final is 50%) or combining group averages.

SAT Trap Alert: The most common trap involving the mean is when the test gives you a frequency table and asks for the mean. Students often take the mean of the values listed rather than weighting by frequency. We will address this in detail in Section 9.5.

9.2.2 — The Median

Definition: The median is the middle value when the data set is arranged in ascending or descending order. It divides the data set into two equal halves.

Finding the Median — Step by Step:

  1. Arrange all values in ascending order.
  2. Count the number of values, $n$.
  3. If $n$ is odd: The median is the value at position $\frac{n+1}{2}$.
  4. Example: For the ordered set ${3, 7, 9, 14, 18}$, $n = 5$, so the median is the $\frac{5+1}{2} = 3\text{rd}$ value, which is $9$.
  5. If $n$ is even: The median is the average of the two middle values, at positions $\frac{n}{2}$ and $\frac{n}{2} + 1$.
  6. Example: For the ordered set ${3, 7, 9, 14}$, $n = 4$, so the median is $\frac{7 + 9}{2} = 8$.

Critical Properties the SAT Tests:

  1. Resistance to outliers. The median is robust. In the set ${2, 3, 4, 5, 100}$, the median is $4$, which accurately reflects the "typical" value. The SAT frequently asks you to choose the better measure of center for a skewed distribution, and the answer is almost always the median.

  2. The median and percentiles. The median is the 50th percentile—exactly 50% of the data falls at or below it. This connects directly to quartiles, which we will cover in Section 9.4.

  3. Median from a frequency table. This is a high-frequency SAT topic. The procedure is:

  4. Calculate the total frequency $N$.
  5. Find the cumulative frequency.
  6. Locate the position $\frac{N+1}{2}$ (for odd $N$) or the positions $\frac{N}{2}$ and $\frac{N}{2}+1$ (for even $N$).
  7. Identify which value in the frequency table corresponds to that position.

Worked Example: Consider the frequency table below:

Score Frequency
50 3
60 7
70 12
80 8
90 5

Total $N = 3 + 7 + 12 + 8 + 5 = 35$. Since $N$ is odd, the median is at position $\frac{35+1}{2} = 18$. Cumulative frequencies: 3 (score 50), 10 (score 60), 22 (score 70). The 18th value falls in the score 70 group, so the median is 70.

9.2.3 — The Mode

Definition: The mode is the value that appears most frequently in a data set.

Key Details:

  • A data set can have one mode (unimodal), two modes (bimodal), more than two modes (multimodal), or no mode if all values appear with equal frequency.
  • The mode is the only measure of center that can be used with categorical (qualitative) data. For example, if a survey asks for favorite color and the most common response is "blue," then blue is the mode.
  • The SAT rarely asks about the mode in isolation but may include it in a question that asks you to compare all three measures of center.

When is each measure most appropriate?

Situation Best Measure Reason
Symmetric distribution, no outliers Mean Uses all data points; most precise
Skewed distribution or outliers present Median Resistant to extreme values
Categorical data Mode Only measure applicable to non-numeric data
Want the "most common" value Mode Directly identifies the most frequent outcome

9.2.4 — Comparing Mean and Median to Determine Shape

This is one of the most tested concepts in SAT statistics. The relationship between the mean and median tells you about the shape of the distribution:

  • Mean = Median: The distribution is approximately symmetric. The data is evenly balanced on both sides.
  • Mean > Median: The distribution is skewed right (positively skewed). A few unusually high values pull the mean to the right. Picture a long tail extending to the right.
  • Mean < Median: The distribution is skewed left (negatively skewed). A few unusually low values pull the mean to the left. Picture a long tail extending to the left.

Memory Aid: The mean follows the tail. The tail pulls the mean. If the tail extends to the right, the mean is to the right of the median (skewed right). If the tail extends to the left, the mean is to the left of the median (skewed left).

The SAT may present a data set and ask you to determine whether it is skewed, or it may describe a real-world scenario (e.g., "home prices in a city where a few mansions exist") and ask you to predict the relationship between mean and median. In the home price example, the distribution is skewed right, so the mean home price exceeds the median home price.

9.3 — Measures of Spread: Range, IQR, and Standard Deviation

While measures of center tell you where the "middle" of the data is, measures of spread tell you how dispersed or spread out the data is. Two data sets can have the same mean but vastly different spreads.

9.3.1 — Range

Definition: The range is the difference between the maximum and minimum values.

$$\text{Range} = \text{Max} - \text{Min}$$

Properties: - It is the simplest but crudest measure of spread. - It is extremely sensitive to outliers. A single extreme value can dramatically inflate the range. - The SAT uses range primarily as a quick check or in combination with other measures.

Example: Data set A: ${4, 5, 5, 6, 6}$ has range $6 - 4 = 2$. Data set B: ${1, 5, 5, 6, 9}$ has range $9 - 1 = 8$. Even though both have the same median (5) and mean (5.2), data set B is clearly more spread out.

9.3.2 — Interquartile Range (IQR)

Definition: The IQR measures the middle 50% of the data. It is the difference between the third quartile ($Q_3$) and the first quartile ($Q_1$).

$$\text{IQR} = Q_3 - Q_1$$

Understanding Quartiles:

  • $Q_1$ (First Quartile / Lower Quartile): The 25th percentile. 25% of the data falls at or below $Q_1$.
  • $Q_2$ (Second Quartile): This is the median (50th percentile).
  • $Q_3$ (Third Quartile / Upper Quartile): The 75th percentile. 75% of the data falls at or below $Q_3$.

How to Find Quartiles:

  1. Order the data from least to greatest.
  2. Find the median ($Q_2$).
  3. $Q_1$ is the median of the lower half of the data (values below $Q_2$).
  4. $Q_3$ is the median of the upper half of the data (values above $Q_2$).
  5. If $n$ is odd, exclude the median itself when finding $Q_1$ and $Q_3$. If $n$ is even, split the data exactly in half.

Worked Example: Find the IQR of ${2, 4, 6, 8, 10, 12, 14, 16, 18}$.

  • $n = 9$, median ($Q_2$) = 10 (the 5th value).
  • Lower half (excluding median): ${2, 4, 6, 8}$. $Q_1 = \frac{4+6}{2} = 5$.
  • Upper half (excluding median): ${12, 14, 16, 18}$. $Q_3 = \frac{14+16}{2} = 15$.
  • $\text{IQR} = 15 - 5 = 10$.

Properties of IQR: - It is resistant to outliers, unlike the range. This is because it focuses on the middle 50% of data, ignoring extreme values in both tails. - It is the basis for identifying outliers using the 1.5 × IQR rule: Any value below $Q_1 - 1.5 \times \text{IQR}$ or above $Q_3 + 1.5 \times \text{IQR}$ is considered a potential outlier. - The SAT may not require you to apply the 1.5 × IQR rule explicitly, but understanding it helps you reason about box plots and unusual values.

9.3.3 — Standard Deviation

Definition: The standard deviation ($\sigma$ for a population, $s$ for a sample) measures the average distance of each data point from the mean. It is the most comprehensive measure of spread because it takes into account every data point.

Conceptual Understanding (What the SAT Tests):

The SAT does not require you to calculate the standard deviation by hand. The formula is complex and the College Board knows this. Instead, the SAT tests your conceptual understanding of what standard deviation means:

  • Large standard deviation → data is spread out from the mean (high variability).
  • Small standard deviation → data is clustered tightly around the mean (low variability).
  • Standard deviation of zero → all values are identical (no variability at all).

What the SAT Asks:

  1. Comparing standard deviations visually. You may be shown two histograms or dot plots and asked which has the larger standard deviation. The one with data more spread out (wider, flatter distribution) has the larger standard deviation.

  2. Effect of transformations. If you add a constant to every value in a data set, the standard deviation does not change (the whole distribution shifts, but the spread stays the same). If you multiply every value by a constant $k$, the standard deviation is multiplied by $|k|$.

  3. Standard deviation and outliers. Adding an outlier to a data set will generally increase the standard deviation.

The Formula (For Reference Only):

$$\sigma = \sqrt{\frac{\sum_{i=1}^{n}(x_i - \bar{x})^2}{n}}$$

You will never need to compute this on the SAT, but understanding that it involves squared deviations from the mean helps you grasp why it is sensitive to outliers (squaring amplifies large deviations).

9.3.4 — Choosing the Right Measure of Spread

Measure Sensitive to Outliers? What It Captures Best Used When
Range Very sensitive Total spread from min to max Quick, rough estimate
IQR Resistant Middle 50% of data Skewed distributions, outliers present
Standard Deviation Sensitive Average distance from mean Symmetric distributions, no extreme outliers

Key SAT Insight: When a data set has outliers or is skewed, the IQR is the preferred measure of spread, just as the median is the preferred measure of center. When the distribution is symmetric and free of outliers, the standard deviation and mean are the preferred pair. These two pairings go together: mean with standard deviation, median with IQR.

9.4 — Graphical Representations and Their Statistical Properties

The SAT frequently presents data in graphical form and asks you to read, compare, or interpret the display. Understanding what each display shows—and what it hides—is essential.

9.4.1 — Dot Plots

A dot plot displays individual data points on a number line, with dots stacked above each value to show frequency.

What they show: Individual data points, exact values, distribution shape, clusters, gaps, and outliers.

What the SAT tests: Reading specific values, counting the number of data points in a range, identifying the mode (tallest stack), and comparing the center and spread of two dot plots.

Example insight: If a dot plot shows most values clustered between 10 and 20, with a few dots at 45 and 50, you can immediately see the distribution is skewed right, and you know the mean will be greater than the median.

9.4.2 — Histograms

A histogram groups data into intervals (bins) and uses bars to show the frequency or count in each interval.

What they show: Distribution shape, approximate center, approximate spread, and the overall pattern of the data.

What they hide: Individual data points. You cannot determine the exact values, and therefore you cannot precisely calculate the mean or median from a histogram—you can only estimate.
